To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au, auxlang{at}listserv{dot}brown{dot}edu
Subject: [glosalist] MAIN GLOSA AUTHOR DIES
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Wed, 05 Jul 2000 01:07:19 +1000

To All at Glosalist and Auxlang,

  Sad news.

  I just received news that Ronald Clark, who had a heart attack last year,
died of a second recently.

  A light in this darkness has been extinguished.

  Now, Wendy Ashby, currently without a Net connection, must decide how,
and if, she will continue the work of promoting Glosa without Ron.  She
needs all the support she can get, and letters of condolence can be sent to
    Wendy Ashby
    PO Box 18, Richmond
    Surry  TW9 1WD
    U. K.

  Ron wished to make a difference to the world, in showing that a Common
Second Language works and is already within our grasp.  I believe he did
leave his mark on history, and also that his "Glosa" is a world class
Auxiliary Language.  Ron Clark will be remembered, but a question mark
hangs over the social invention that he wished to give to the world.
  Will Glosa become a major component of the Better World we all seek?
  Through renewed effort, those who value the language, and share Ron's
dream for a Communicating World, will work with Wendy to create a new
chapter in the Glosa saga.

  Ave Ronald.

Robin Gaskell
 &&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&
A Holo-pe de Glosalist e Auxlang,

  Es fo tristi nova.

  Mi nu-pa gene u nova ke Ronald Clark; qi pa sufere u kardia noku pa anua,
pa recento morta ex u bi noku.

  U brila foto intra u-ci skoto pa gene ge-sto.

  Nu, Wendy Ashby, presenti sine un Inter-reti nexu, nece decide per qo
mode, e si, fe fu kontinu un ergo de promoti Glosa, sine Ron.  Fe nece gene
holo u suporta; qi na pote don a fe; e plu grama de kondole pote gene
ge-bali a
    Wendy Ashby
    PO Box 18, Richmond
    Surry  TW9 1WD
    U. K.

  Ron pa volu kausa u difere ad u munda, per monstro ke u Komuni Sekunda
Lingua funktio; e ke id nu es intra na manu.  Mi kredi: an pa loka an marka
epi histori; e pluso, ke an "Glosa" eqa un auxi-lingua de munda klasi.  Ron
Clark fu gene ge-memo, sed u qestio-marka pende supra u socia inventi; qi
an pa vo don ad u munda.
  Qe, Glosa fu es u major mero de u Ma Boni Munda; qi holo-na cerka?
  Per ge-re-nova eforti, plu-pe; qi valu u lingua, e qi logi Ron sonia de u
Du-komunika Munda, fu ergo ko Wendy te krea u neo kapitula de u Glosa histori.

  Ave Ronald.

Robin Gaskell

*   Dear Alexander,

    Thank you for your e-mail.  This IS the right question.  Unfortunately, I do not have a completely satisfactory answer.


ANSWER:

 1. My URL  www.zeta.org.au/~rgaskell  has a lot of stuff on it, but no

instruction book, as such.

 2. I will send by separate message a Redirected message carrying other URLs for Glosa.

 3. As an attachment to this e-mail, I add the "Glosa Classified" file;

this has words grouped into collections with similar function.  It is an old file with some out-of-date addresses.

 4. I offer to send you a printed copy of "18 Steps to Euro-Glosa", which is a very basic introductory instruction book, written by the Glosa authors - please send your land address if you would like this.

 5. I will send an IBM 3.5 inch floppy disk with various Glosa files on it, if you would like it - again, land address needed.

 6. There is one cassette that I have made up using spoken Glosa together with printed Glosa/English sheets.  I would rather send this as a sale item: cost = replacement cost of blank cassette; minimum payment an Australian $5 note from a Russian bank; but if this is not possible, FREE.


 #7. I have been planning, for some time, to prepare a Glosa instruction course based on a successful English-language course, that uses cassette

instruction.  Your request might signal the need for me to start this project.  If you are interested in Alpha-testing such a course, I would treat you as a part author in the project; the cost of postage of materials, to you, would be bourne by me.



  So, I welcome your request, and it could stimulate me to action.  Setting up a cassette-based instruction course for Glosa is well overdue.

  It would, however be paper/cassette based and rely on normal postage for distribution.

  In theory, a suitable introductory instruction course shuld be available through the Internet: I will have to think about this!


Bien-venu a Glosa. Saluta,

[Welcome to Glosa. Greetings.]


Robin Gaskell

   Here is  G/ *  Glosa . Classified  - categories of words (1000 list)

        *** This is a `lift' [scanned] from a Richmond publication ... as
        such, it would be better /for the authors/ for you to actually
        order, and pay for, such information from Glosa Central. ***

        >>> However, I am treating you as a `friendly' and a potential

 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

Page ONE
 __________________________________________________________________________
|                          ENGLISH - GLOSA 1000                            |
|                             CLASSIFIED                                   |
|__________________________________________________________________________|
 __________________________________________________________________________
|                         INTERNATIONAL LANGUAGE                           |
|__________________________________________________________________________|
 _________________________________________________________________________
|                                                                          |
|      "The world-wide Vocabulary of Science is the nearest thing to       |
| the Lexicon of a truly Global Language that Mankind has yet achieved.    |
| It derives it's words from two dead languages - Latin, and now almost    |
| exclusively Greek."                                                      |
|                      -Prof. Lancelot Hogben                              |
|                                                                          |
|      "We can see in this growing Scientific Nomenclature an adumbration  |
|  of the International Language of Tomorrow."                             |
|                      -Prof. Simeon Potter                                |
|__________________________________________________________________________|



Page 2

                        INTRODUCTION

        * During the past 2 centuries Scientists have created a
world-wide technical Vocabulary.  As Modern Technology transforms
everyday life what was once the Vocabulary of the Laboratory becomes the
Vocabulary of the Street Corner.

        * Glosa has no grammer.  About 20 particles do all the work of
Tense, Number, Comparison, etc.

        * Below Glosa 1000 is divided into useful categories.

        * It is important to understand that this is no mere `get-by'
language, but a complete language able to deal with any theme as
demonstrated in the periodical PGN, Plu Glosa Nota.

        * Glosa words suffer no inflexion.  You take them straight from
the Dictionary and put them into sentences.

        * Glosa is a semantically based language. i.e. a group of words
which share a common element of meaning should show this in their form.
For example, an aviary, library, cattle-shed, theatre, stable, and
church, and many others all have a common element, in that they are all
buildings.  The Greek and Latin for a building contains the root DOM- as
in DOMestic, DOMIcile.  In Glosa any building ends with the generic -DO a
shorter form of DOMI.  In Glosa a library is a BIBLI-DO, a book-house, a
theatre a DRAMA-DO.  These Generics are listed below and enable you to
build up a very large vocabulary without having to memorize a great
number of words.
        Similarly, opposites in Glosa are formed by pre-positing NO- or
NON- before a vowel.  Thus ALTI, high as in ALTItude can be brought down
in NON-ALTI, low.

        * It is important to understand that each CONCEPT word stands for
an idea, and so corresponds somewhat to a Head-Word in Roget's Thesaurus,
standing for a great number of English words sharing the same idea.
Context gives precision.  The IndoEuropean languages all have far too
many homosemes, words meaning roughly the same.  This puts a great load
on the memory of a student; and this mistake is not repeated in Glosa.

        * All words in a Glosa Dictionary are to be found in any good
English Dictionary such as OED or Websters.  They also of course occur in
all other Euro-Dictionaries, French, German, and Russian, and outside
Europe in most oriental dictionaries such as Japanese and Malaysian.

Copyright: Wendy Ashby and Ronald Clark, 1992            GLOSA
                                                      P.O. Box 18
                                                        RICHMOND
                                                     SURREY TW9 2AU
                                                        ENGLAND


Page 3

______________________________________________________________________________
GLOSA 1000 - CLASSIFIED...
______________________________________________________________________________
_________________________________________________________________
QUESTIONS                                                        |
_________________________________________________________________|

yes/no questions.................begin with Qe...?
What/Which?......................Qo?
How many?........................Qo numera?
How much?........................Qo metri?
How often?.......................Qo freqe?
Why(CAuse).......................Qo-ka?
Why(inTEnt)......................Qo-te?
Where?...........................Qo-lo?
What thing?......................Qo-ra?
Who?.............................Qo-pe?
How?.............................Qo-mode or Komo

_______________________________________________________________
PRONOUNS                                                       |
_______________________________________________________________|

I, me, my........................mi
you..............................tu
she, her.........................fe
he, him, his.....................an
it, it's.........................id
one, one's.......................pe
we, us, our......................na
you, your........................vi
they, them, their................mu
self.............................se
each other.......................alelo

________________________________________________________________
ARTICLES                                                        |
________________________________________________________________|

a, the...........................u, un
all, every.......................pan(to)
any..............................ali
each.............................singu
enough of........................sati
few..............................oligo
big, much, lot...................mega
many, a lot of...................multi, poli
more than one....................plu
a little.........................pusi
only.............................solo
a bit of.........................mero, peci
this (the-here)..................u-ci
that (the-there).................u-la
these/those......................plu-ci/plu-la
a kind of........................speci
the whole of.....................holo
which?...........................qo?

                           PRONUNCIATION

        Every letter must be pronounced including final vowel.
        5 vowels as in English: fAther, cafE, machIne, mOre, lUnar.
        Consonants as in English, with the following exceptions:
        hard C always represented by Glosa K.
        Glosa C stands for CH in English CHurCH.
        J as Y in Yes.
        R must always be pronounced, preferably trilled.
        ACCENT gently on vowel before last consonant.
        SPELLING is phonetic: Greek CH replaced by K, PH by F, Y by I.

dear Robin
have been thinking about a Latin/Glosa dic for a while. Instead of sending
word lists in umpteen different languages, we could use Latin as most
european people could get hold of a latin/own tongue without too much bother
and could move from own to latin to glosa. I suggest that this applies only
to G1000..... and that further dictionaries would have G8004 etc defined in
G1000. I did start one so I'll try to rake it up. I think G1000 is perhaps a
bit restricting although the idea is good and we might perhaps aim for
something like G3000. There seems to be a site about dictionaries but I
haven't dabbled yet. If someone has already done something in latin I'd be
interested.
have communicated with alexander - more to follow
syd

From: "Jay Bowks" <jjbowks{at}adam{dot}cheshire{dot}net>
Date: Thu, 15 Jul 1999 14:57:40 -0400

Ameslan is American Sign Language.
I'm one of the two translators at our Kingdom
Hall on Sundays that helps out our deaf friends
enjoy the program.

The fingerspelling alphabet used in Ameslan
goes back many centuries and is based on
French and Spanish monks who used it
to communicate as they had taken a vow of
silence, imagine that, and now deaf people
all over the United States use this manual
alphabet to fingerspell.

I have a couple of fonts for windows which
you can use in any windows wordprocessor
to spell out things with hands on your screen.
It's pretty neat stuff. I've also used the signs
to teach Spanish pronunciation to my students.

If you'd like the fonts I'll email them to you
privately on a message, so as not to get the list
on a tangent :-)

Since,
Jay B.

At 15:09 09/07/2000 +0400, Alexander Kirpichev wrote:

>Saluta!

>I have seen your URLs. Thank you! Now I trying to learn the language. Do

>my Glosa is correct?

>Mi pa du vi URLs. Gratia! Na du mi trenta gene sko u lingua.

>Qe mi Glosa es veri?

>Vale!

>

Hi Alexander and other Glosalist-pe,

  Alexander your Glosa is understandable, and will get into proper form (syntax) as you go along.

  You are using the ^jump into the deep end of the pool^ method of learning Glosa.  And this is possibly the most valid ways of adding Glosa to your thinking.

1. ~Mi pa du vi URLs.~   ... "VERB" missing: vide = (to) see

Essential:-

a. ~Mi pa du vide vi URL.~

   [I did continue to see your(<<- plural) URL]

    I have been seeing your URL.

Alternative:-

a'. ~Mi pa vide tu plu URL.~

    [I di see your(<<- singular) (more than one)URL]

     I saw your URLs.

    >> This corrects for number, and puts the action out of continuous tense and into the simple past. <<<<


2. ~Na du mi trenta gene sko u lingua.~   ... spelling corrections

Essential:-

b.  ~Nu mi du tenta gene sko u lingua.~

    [now I continue to try to learn the language]

     Now I am trying to learn the language.

Possible:-

b'. ~Nu mi tenta gene sko de u lingua.~

    [now I try  to get learning of the language]

     Now I (will) try to learn the language.


3. ~Qe mi Glosa es veri?   ... concept-word selection: veri = (to be) true

   [question: my Glosa is true]

    Is my Glosa true?

Better:-

c'. ~Qe, mi Glosa habe korekti?~

    [(does) my Glosa have correctness]

     Is my Glosa correct?


  So, there you go Alexander.  This is quite a good effort for starting on a new language.

Overall Hints:

  ! Aim to 'think in Glosa' as soon as possible.

  @ As a "concept language" you can ask yourself:

      * is this the right word to convey my idea?

      * am I translating an idiom from a national language?

  # Check for "feel"; when you read yor sentence out loud:

      * does it bring your originally intended meaning back into mind?

      * does the srtring of words sound pleasant?

      * is the written sentence appealing to the eye?

         % If the answer to any of these questions is, "NO", then it is

             probably better to recast the sentence.


  By the way, I am happy that you have discovered Glosa, and that you are

checking the language out through practicing it.

  As your Glosa fluency improves, I will ask how you compare expressing yourself in Russian and in Glosa.


Saluta,


Robin Gaskell

   P.S.  Below are plain-text attachments Seminars #1 & #2; I think you will find them helpful.
--=====================_963704741==_
Content-Type: text/plain; charset="us-ascii"
Content-Disposition: attachment; filename="Seminar.#1"

_A first Seminar on Glosa____________________________R. Gaskell Sept,1995_
               (With apologies, in English)
WORDS
        Words in Glosa represent concepts, and are really `concept/words.'
They represent ideas, but, depending on their position in the sentence,
and their relationships to other words, they may represent that idea in its
solid form (noun), active form (verb), or as a modifier of another idea
(adjective, adverb).

        The words do not change for reasons of grammar (system for getting
understanding from words): this means that words can be used, within
reason, as any part of speech, but have no letters added to indicate this
useage (no Part-of-Speech markers); also, the words which act as verbs,
`verbs', have no endings added to them (no inflections) to give added
understanding of how, or when, the action is taken.

        The order of the words (syntax) gives us the meaning in Glosa; the
language can be described as having `syntax-based grammar.'

PHRASES
        The phrase (small group of words) is the basic unit of sentence
construction in Glosa.  A small group of words might include a thing and
some description of it (noun phrase), or the group might include an action
and some modifications to this action (verb phrase).  A small group of
words at the start of a Glosa sentence might qualify the action of the
sentence (adverbial phrase).

        Within each phrase, each word is modified by the one before it, and
there is a gradual increase in importance of the words, with the main
concept/word (`noun' or `verb') as the last word of the phrase.
                e.g.  the three fast, loud red cars                  NP
                      plu tri celero fo-sono rubi vagona

                e.g.  were quickly end excitedly talking loudly      VP
                      pa du celero e excita fo-sono dice

                e.g.  while running     (relates to  --->  verb)     AdvP
                      tem kursi

        In speech, phrases are said as one group, often with a raising then
lowering of intonation (pitch), from start to finish; in written form, if
it seems necessary for the understanding of meaning, the start and finish
of phrases, is indicated using punctuation (commas).

CLAUSES
        A clause is a larger group of words containing two (NP + VP) or
more phrases, and such a simple two or three phrase clause can make up the
whole sentence (major clause).  However, a clause - which always includes
at least one verb phrase - might add meaning to the action of the
sentence (Adverbial Clause), might give more description to one of the
things in a sentence (Adjectival Clause), or it could be a group of words,
including a `verb,' used in the place that you would normally find a noun
phrase (Noun Clause).

      e.g.  The boy arrived.              U ju-an pa ariva.            MC

      e.g.  The girl went home.           U ju-fe pa ki a fe domi.     MC

      e.g.  The man will eat the meal.    Un andra fu vora u vora.     MC

      e.g.  She spoke so loudly that she was heard.
            Fe pa ta sono dice ke fe pa gene ge-audi.                AdvC
                          ~~~~ <-- ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

      e.g.  The girl went home after she had eaten a good meal.
            U ju-fe pa ki a fe domi, po fe pra vora u boni vora.     AdvC
                       ~~   <---     ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
      e.g.  The boy, who was fat and badly dressed, arrived.
            U ju-an; qi pa es paki e mali ge-vesti, pa ariva.        AdjC
              ~~~~~ <-- ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
      e.g.  I like to get away from the house.
                  (getting)
            Mi amo gene ab u domi.                                     NC
                   ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
      e.g.  The farmer hoped that it would rain.
            Un agri-pe pa spera: ke id sio pluvi.                      NC
                       ~~~(V)~~  ~~~~(O)~~~~~~~~

SEQUENCE IN A CLAUSE
        The normal sequence in Glosa is the same as in English:-
 Main Clause =   noun phrase    -    verb phrase     -    noun phrase
                  (SUBJECT)            (VERB)              (OBJECT)
            Doer of the action    Action being done    Thing acted upon
           e.g.     He                 posts               a letter.
                    An                 posta               u grama.

        There are three main variations to this: clauses with no object
(intransitive verb); clauses where, for reasons of style, the speaker,
or writer, wishes to emphasise the object by placing it first (Passive
Voice); and Noun Clauses which have no subject (infinitive verb).
           e.g.    She (NP)    sang. (VP)                 Fe pa kanta.
                 (SUBJECT)    (VERB)

           e.g.   The letter       was posted        by the man.
                   U grama      pa gene ge-posta     ex un andra.
                               (did get got-post)
                   (OBJECT)         (VERB)                (SUBJECT)

           e.g.                  - running (=to run)     on wet grass
                                    kursi              epi ge-hidra gra
                                    (VERB)                 (OBJECT)

        Because its grammar depends on the word order, Glosa works best if
we keep to the normal (S-V-O) structure in clauses.  In poetry and fiction,
however, the stylistic variations are necessary.

FUNCTION WORDS
        These are the small words that are not concept/words, but which
join these words together (conjunctions), lead us into a phrase or clause
(prepositions), create negatives, introduce questions, or modify the timing
of an action when placed before a `verb' (tense particles).  There are also
the small words placed before `nouns' to tell us that they are things, and
how many, or how much, of them there are (determinants).
        Joining words:  e, sed, pluso, alo, ni

        Prepositions: tem, a, ex, de, anti, seqe, po, pre, vice, krom ....

        Negatives: ne, nuli, no-, ni ... ni, nuli-

        Questions: Qe, qestio, qod, qo-

        Tense particles: pa, fu, du, nu, pra, sio, ge-, gene

        Before `nouns': u, plu, plura, uno, vario, poli, oligo, u mero de
         (determinant)

SENTENCES
        Putting all the above together, in Glosa, as in English, sentences
can be made up of various combinations of phrases and clauses - as long as
the Subject-Verb-Object rule is observed.  In Glosa, also, there is the
added requirement that words in phrases follow the rule of increasing
importance, with the main concept/word last.

        If the sentence is made up of phrases only, there is a single
`verb', and the sentence is described as a Major Clause.  If there are two
or more `verbs', then the `verb' describing the main action forms the Main
Clause, and the other clauses (NC, AdvC, AdjC) are called Subordinate
Clauses.  The whole sentence can, however, still be called the Major
Clause.
        The secret of good sentence formation is in using well-formed
phrases to make up the sentences and their constituent clauses.

PRECEDENCE IN PHRASES
RULE: In a phrase, a word is modified by the word in front of it (its
        precedent.)
RULE: In a normal sentence (Major Clause), there is a tendency for the
        first noun phrase (SUBJECT) to modify the verb phrase (VERB), and
        then, for the verb phrase to modify the second noun phrase (OBJECT).
RULE: A modifying clause (Adjectival or Adverbial) follows directly after
        the concept/word that it modifies; this structure is indicated
        by the placing of a semi-colon (;) or possibly a colon (:) before
        the new clause.

 In a noun phrase, the sequence is:-

           determinant  -  number  -  quantity  -  modifier(s)  -  noun

        e.g. plu tri no-ge-numera, fo sono, no-puri ju-an
             the three uncounted, shouting, dirty boys

           Note.  The concept/word used as a noun is the only element of
              a noun phrase that is essential.

 In a verb phrase, the sequence is:-
                                                    |   verb
  negative - tense - modifier(s) - auxiliary verb - |      OR
 particle                                           | verboid + amplifier

                     e.g. ne pa hedo, no-soni tenta | kursi
                                                    | ki ana

                       did not happily, quietly try | to run
                                                    | to go up

           Note, again.  The concept/word used as a verb is the only
               element of a verb phrase that is essential.

 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

_A second Seminar on Glosa____________________________R. Gaskell Sept,1998_
-----------------------------______________________------- File: SEMINAR.#2

BASIC GRAMMAR RULES
                     There are three basic rules behind the grammar of
Glosa: combined, they tell us 'position determines function'.

        1. S-V-O: Subject, Verb, Object order in a clause.
        2. "A word is modified by its precedent."  One word modifies the
             next, and so on.
        3. A word can function, within reason, as any part of speech.
             There are no Part-of-Speech markers, but a word's function is
             indicated by its position in a sentence.
        __________________________________________________________________
        All other rules of Glosa usage follow from these.
All sentences contain a 'verb': it is the word that tells us the action
occurring in the sentence; it gives the sentence _life_.

VERBS
        In Glosa, the word in the middle of the sentence, describing the
action, is the 'verb' ('verb' = word functioning as a verb).

  EG  U kani bibe u hidra.  ...................  bibe  'verb'
             ~~~~
   [The dog drinks the water.]

  EG  An fo hedo bibe.  .......................  hedo  'verb'
            ~~~~   ............................  bibe  'gerund' (verb/noun)
   [He very-much enjoys to drink/drinking.]

  EG  Mi don ad un equs id bibe.  .............  don(a)  'verb'
         ~~~        ...........................  bibe  'noun'
   [I give (to) the horse its drink.]

N.B. 'verb' =  word functioning as a verb
     'noun' =  word functioning as a noun
      ' X ' =  word functioning as an  X

"IS" IN ENGLISH
                 This word is used in many different ways in English, but,
in Glosa, the "is" concept is conveyed more accurately - using a range of
'verbs'.  As an 'action' its original function was to represent an "equals"
sign, EG "He is John."  Glosa translations for various uses of "is" in
English are:-

  es  -  to be, is (has equivalence with), has the essence of
  habe - has the property (quality) of
  gene - gets to be, becomes
  eqa - equals, measures

Examples:
    Fe es u ju-fe.
   [She is a girl.]
    An es u filosofi-pe.
   [He is a philosopher.]

    U meteo habe frigo.
   [The weather is cold/has coldness] [It is cold.]
    Mi habe fo lati.
   [I am very tall/have much tallness.]

    U meteo gene ma termo.
   [The weather is warmer/gets warmer/becomes more hot.]
    U ju-an gene fatiga.
   [The boy is tired/becomes tired.]
    Mi habe fo turba; e fu gene koleri, si tu ne sto dice.
   [I am very upset, and will be angry if you don't stop talking.]

    U temperatura eqa tri-ze grada (30`C).
   [The temperature is thirty degrees (30`C).]
    U preci de ki-in u teatra eqa mo-penta dolara ($15).
   [The price of entry into the theatre is fifteen dollars ($15).]

COMMON 'VERBS'
                In Glosa, some words are used almost exclusively as verbs,
and these are general-purpose verbs, such as:-

  habe - to have, have has
  ki  -  to go, go, goes, went
  veni - to come, come, comes, came
  tena - to hold, grab, take

VERBOIDS
          Some words, in Glosa, are "almost verbs" but are not "full verbs"
in their own right: they are not 'stand-alone' verbs, but they add to the
meaning of the "full verb", which is usually the final word in the Verb
Phrase.  These Verboids are half-way between Adverbs and Verbs.  Examples:-

  posi - possibly
  pote - can, has the ability to
  nece - needs to, must
  lice - be allowed, allow to
  debi - ought to
  hedo - enjoys, happily
  forti - strongly
  seqe - following, sebsequently
  vo(lu) - wish to, would
  tanto - so much, almost
  tosto - soon

EG  Mi fu posi ki ad u boteka.
   [I will possibly go to the shop.]
    An pote kurso fo celero.
   [He can run very quickly.]
    Kron mi voka, fe tosto veni in u domi.
   [When I call, she soon comes into the house.]
    Mi gina fu hedo kanta pro vi.
   [My wife will happily sing for you.]
    Tu lice habe id.
   [You are allowed to have it.]
    Pa noktu, mi tanto ariva pre u pluvi.  .. (Pastness implied by "Pa" so
   [Last night I almost arrived before the rain.]  'pa tanto ariva' wrong.)

AUXILIARY VERBS
                 These words 'help' the verb to do its job.  But they
usually follow the "main verb" as separate words, though they contribute to
the formation of a "compound verb."  Examples are:-

  ana - up   EG  ki ana  [ascend]
  kata - down   EG  ki kata  [descend]
  retro - back   EG  kurso retro  [run back]
  itera - again   EG  akti itera  [do (it) again; repeat]
  ab  -  away   EG  greso ab  [walk away]
  versi - back to start   EG  viagia versi  [travel home]
  avanti - forward   EG  ki avanti  [advance]

EG  An pa ki kata un orto-lo; sed an nece skende itera ana id, te
   [He fell down the cliff, but (he) must climb again up it, in order to

    gene an kefa-ve; qi veni ab an kefa pre an pa kade.
    get his hat, which came off his head before he fell.]

WAYS IN WHICH THE 'VERB' MAY BE MODIFIED
  In Glosa, the word functioning as the verb can be modified by placing
short words, called particles, before it.  These particles might alter the
time of the event, negate it, turn it back on the Subject, or make it
conditional on some other event.  As well as that, the action might act
upon - or not act upon - an Object; and then, for a change, the
positioning Subject and Object is sometimes switched around.

. TENSE PARTICLES
                   Dealt with, in detail - in the Tense Table - these short
words, which come at the start of a Verb Phrase, usually singly but
sometimes in a group, tell us WHEN an action occurs.

     - No particle before the 'verb' suggest a present tense (but can mean
        that an earlier time indication logically applies).
 pa  -  Simple Past Tense  (pa  [did])
 fu  -  Future Tense  (fu  [will])
 nu  -  An immediate Present Tense  (nu  [just now])
 du  -  Continuous Tense   (du X  [is X-ing, continues to X])
 pra -  Past Perfect Tense: fully completed.   (pra  [had])

  EG  Un andra nu pa veni in u domi.
               ~~~~~
     [The man just now came into the house.]

CONDITIONAL
             When an action happens only if something else occurs before
it, then we say that the second event is conditional on the first: in
English, this is usually shown by the use of "would", "should" or "could".

 sio  -  would
 sio debi - would ought, should
 sio posi - would possibly, could

  EG  Si mu gene pluvi, plu-ci sperma sio posi kresce in alti fito.
     [If they get rain, these plants could grow into tall bushes.]

NEGATIVE
          When an action does NOT happen, we indicate this using a negative
word before the verb.  This happens in English, and something very similar
occurs in Glosa.

 ne  -  does not
 no  -  un-
 nuli - never  (also used with 'nouns':  nuli ra  [no thing])
 ni X ni Y - neither X nor Y

  EG  U gina ne ki a plu boteka; ka fe vagona ne ergo.
     [The woman did not go to the shops, because her car did not work.]

REFLEXIVE
            When an action affects the person or thing that caused it, we
say that the action is a "reflexive" action.  In Glosa there are two
particles that are used after the 'verb' to indicate that the action is
received by the Subject of the sentence: one if the Subject is human, the
other for non-human Subjects:-

 -se  -  to itself, itself
 auto -  to himself/herself, himself/herself, themselves

  EG  Jon vagona frakto-se, tem an du age ad an ergo-lo.
     [Jon's car broke itself while he was driving to work (his work-place).]

      Kron fe skende kata plu grada; fe pa kade, e noku auto.
     [When she descended the stairs, she fell and hurt herself.]

TRANSITIVE/
        /INTRANSITIVE   While the standard English sentence, has two Noun
Phrases separated by a Verb Phrase, some sentences have no real Object -
receiver of the action: these are called "intransitive" sentences. Glosa
also follows this pattern:-

  Transitive  S-V-O   EG  U kani fago u karni.  [The dog eats the meat.]
  Intransitive  S-V   EG  U kani voka.    [the dog barks.]
  Intransitive  S-V(-IO)  EG   U kani voka ad u posta-pe.
   IO --> Indirect Object     [The dog barks at the post-man.]

PASSIVE
 VOICE   While most of English and Glosa has the normal structure where
         the thing doing the action comes first, and whatever receives the
action comes last (called Active Voice), both languages allow the reciever
of the action to get a mention at the start of the sentence (called Passive
Voice).  It can be thought of as an (O-) V (-S) sentence:-

 X V Y  -  X does action V to Y   EG  U kani morda un andra.
  .................................  [The dog bites the man.]

 Y gets V-ed by X  -  Y has action V done to it by X
  EG  Un andra gene ge-morda ex u kani.
     [The man gets bitten by the dog.]

From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>

1st quote:
> >have been thinking about a Latin/Glosa dic for a while. Instead of sending
> >word lists in umpteen different languages, we could use Latin as most
> >european people could get hold of a latin/own tongue without too much bother

2nd quote:
> 1. Latin has very hard grammar and orthography, and maybe necessary a "Other
> artificial language"-Glosa dic? I have seen in Internet Ido-Glosa
> dictionary, but Ido is not most popular artificial language in the world.
> In the future after Russian/glosa I want make Esperanto-Glosa.

I made the Ido-Glosa comparison, to demonstrate/discover
that their basic roots are quite similar. It is at:
http://catty.com/~catty/lang/idoglosa.htm

I think the idea of a multi-lingual dictionary is good.
Occidental and Novial both have such dictionaries
(really they are just fancy word lists) but neither
is on the web in a complete state. See:
http://www.cs.brown.edu/~dpb/novial/nl.html

I have made many such word lists by combining others
found on the web. Here is a sample, words beginning
in "but..." in English, Glosa, Ido, and Lingua Franca:

glo     buta            eng     purpose
glo     butalis         eng     nightingale
eng     butcher         glo     macela
eng     butcher         glo     trucido
lf      butia           eng     bottle
ido     butik           eng     shop, store, boutique
glo     butina          eng     booty
glo     butiro          eng     butter
ido     buton           eng     button
glo     butona          eng     button
ido     butr            eng     butter
eng     butter          glo     butiro
eng     butter          ido     butr
eng     butter          lf      mantega
eng     buttercup       glo     ranunkulus
eng     butterfly       glo     lepidoptera
eng     butterfly       glo     papilio
eng     butterfly       ido     papilion
eng     buttocks        glo     pigi
eng     buttocks, rump  glo     glutea
eng     button          glo     butona
eng     button          ido     buton
eng     button          lf      botone

I think such lists are useful in designing an auxlang to
avoid "false friends" and pick tuly international words.
So, I think adding Latin and Greek equivalents would be
a very useful thing to do.

Taking as an example "butterfly", there are at least
two other good international words other than Glosa's
scientific choice "lepidoptera": "mariposa" and "papilion".
I have come to think this is unavoidable and OK; it does
increase the vocabulary so it is more difficult to learn.

But it is more honest and Glosa has done a good thing by
classifying words into categories, something that only
the Lingua Franca Nova auxlang has emulated or pioneered:
http://www.ship.edu/~cgboeree/lfnintro.html

I would propose that we do something like this:
make a 3-column list in several languages.
When multiple words are equivalent, list each
on a separate line, so that a sorted list
is easier to use, and columns are kept narrow.
There should be 50 to 100 different "categories".

category    Glosa          English
==========  =============  ===========
animal	    lepidoptera    butterfly
clothing    butona         button
occupation  macela         butcher
occupation  trucido        butcher

Then, such lists could be merged/combined by a program
to make customized lists, such as Glosa/English/Russian
or Glosa/French/Latin or Glosa/Ido/Novial/Latin/Greek,
posted on the web, and frequently corrected and updated
by all who wish to contribute their efforts. There are
some web-updated multi-lingual dictionaries already,
but not for auxlangs, and they are not systematic.

At 14:02 16/07/2000 EDT, Syd wrote:

>
*   Alexander added that Glosa, like Latin would act as a rough
communication medium for Europeans who knew only their Euro-language.
    And both Jay and Charles have been working on translating lists to
allow translation between Euro-languages and Auxiliary languages.

    It has long been my thought that Glosa, or an abbreviated sub-set of
it, would function, quite well, as a META-language for information booths
at international events .. like the coming Sydney Olympic Games.  But don't
worry, I have not approached the organising committee with any such
suggestions.

    But, for me, such do-gooding ideas are far in the future, considering
how long it is taking just to make headway with the Language, Glosa, on its
own.  I have no time to go off at tangents, at present.  Basically I am
working hard at the political level to prevent Australia from suffering a
fate, similar to the one that Alexander reports, as having befallen him in
Russia.

Saluta,
  Robin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] tutor - thoughts about dictionary
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Tue, 18 Jul 2000 15:13:07 +1000

Caro Plu Glosa-pe,
  Ci mi itera grafo a vi.

At 09:40 16/07/2000 -0700, Charles wrote:
>1st quote:(Syd)
>> >have been thinking about a Latin/Glosa dic for a while. Instead of sending
>> >word lists in umpteen different languages, we could use Latin as most
>> >european people could get hold of a latin/own tongue without too much
bother
>
>2nd quote:(Alex.)
>> 1. Latin has very hard grammar and orthography, and maybe necessary a
"Other
>> artificial language"-Glosa dic? I have seen in Internet Ido-Glosa
>> dictionary, but Ido is not most popular artificial language in the world.
>> In the future after Russian/glosa I want make Esperanto-Glosa.
>
>I made the Ido-Glosa comparison, to demonstrate/discover
>that their basic roots are quite similar. It is at:
>http://catty.com/~catty/lang/idoglosa.htm
>
*  Yes they must be similar if they have common source languages.

>I think the idea of a multi-lingual dictionary is good.
>Occidental and Novial both have such dictionaries
>(really they are just fancy word lists) but neither
>is on the web in a complete state. See:
>http://www.cs.brown.edu/~dpb/novial/nl.html
>
>I have made many such word lists by combining others
>found on the web. Here is a sample, words beginning
>in "but..." in English, Glosa, Ido, and Lingua Franca:
>
>glo     buta            eng     purpose
>glo     butalis         eng     nightingale
>eng     butcher         glo     macela
>    .....
>eng     buttercup       glo     ranunkulus
>eng     butterfly       glo     lepidoptera
>eng     butterfly       glo     papilio
>eng     butterfly       ido     papilion
>    .....
>I think such lists are useful in designing an auxlang to
>avoid "false friends" and pick tuly international words.
>So, I think adding Latin and Greek equivalents would be
>a very useful thing to do.
>
*   Agreed, to avoid unwanted homophones, this is a good idea.
    Sorting through dictionaries to find "commonality" was something that
Ron Clark was doing - selecting Glosa vocabulary - in 1977, when I met him.
    So, long prior to my joining discussion on the Net, I had already
assumed that the sorting work had all been done.  It was only when I
''reversed'' the Glosa 6000 dictionary (converting the Gl.-->Eng. list to
one showing Eng.-->Gl.), that I found Ron and Wendy had included many
unnecessary Classical synonyms, mainly Greek ones.
    Had the situation not gone beyond one Glosa word from the Greek, and
one derived from Latin, for single English words, I could have coped.
  EG water:  hidra G., aqa L.

>Taking as an example "butterfly", there are at least
>two other good international words other than Glosa's
>scientific choice "lepidoptera": "mariposa" and "papilion".
>I have come to think this is unavoidable and OK; it does
>increase the vocabulary so it is more difficult to learn.
>
*   This to me is a clear-cut case, and not one where fashion, or word
frequency, plays a part in the selection of the Glosa word.
    In my introduction to Glosa, I was told that it was a language for
science, and I still feel this to be fundamentally so.  Some centuries ago
scientists chose "Lepioptera" as the group name for butterflies, and it
would seem counter-productive to suggest breaking this Glosa/science
association now: I know that ~lepidoptera~ is longer, and less melodious,
than ~papilio~; and, though both words are in _Glosa 60000_, I still see
the scientific name as the prime word, with ~papilio~ as the synonym
available for stylistic and poetic purposes.

>But it is more honest and Glosa has done a good thing by
>classifying words into categories, something that only
>the Lingua Franca Nova auxlang has emulated or pioneered:
>http://www.ship.edu/~cgboeree/lfnintro.html
>
*   Yes this was a neat idea: I feel that Ron could see people were having
trouble in letter-writing, having to sort through the dictionary,
alphabetically, for every word.  The grouping of the various categories of
function words was, I feel, a particularly smart move.

>I would propose that we do something like this:
>make a 3-column list in several languages.
>When multiple words are equivalent, list each
>on a separate line, so that a sorted list
>is easier to use, and columns are kept narrow.
>There should be 50 to 100 different "categories".
>
>category    Glosa          English
>==========  =============  ===========
>animal	    lepidoptera    butterfly
>clothing    butona         button
>occupation  macela         butcher
>occupation  trucido        butcher
>
*   However, to do this for every Glosa word would, I suspect, be
unnecessary, sometimes misleading, and possibly restrictive.
    Where homonyms occur, in either Glosa or English, I would see such
function/category statements as useful,  EG
       bear (to hold) -        tena
            (to give birth) -  nati
            (animal)  -        ursus

>Then, such lists could be merged/combined by a program
>to make customized lists, such as Glosa/English/Russian
>or Glosa/French/Latin or Glosa/Ido/Novial/Latin/Greek,
>posted on the web, and frequently corrected and updated
>by all who wish to contribute their efforts. There are
>some web-updated multi-lingual dictionaries already,
>but not for auxlangs, and they are not systematic.
>
*   In the days of Early Glosa, Ron Clark (c. 1979) did form a multi
language dictionary with English, French, German and either Spanish or
Italian.  Nowadays the Glosa would not be the same, because it underwent
numerous small changes in the 80's.
    For the moment, I have no time or energy for the suggested compilations
Charles suggests, even though the Cyrillic and Greek scripts are catered
for in multi-lingual word processors.
    Finding agreements between the various Planned Languages, in the long
run, could be the hardest task of all, because their lexicons can appear
like ^shifting sands^.
    However, with retirement looming, and the fuuture somewhat uncertain, I
will concentrate all of my linguistic efforts solely on the promoting and
use of Glosa.

Saluta,
  Robin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] death, dictionary, diplomacy
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Tue, 18 Jul 2000 11:06:56 -0700

Robin Gaskell wrote:

>     It has long been my thought that Glosa, or an abbreviated sub-set of
> it, would function, quite well, as a META-language for information booths
> at international events .. like the coming Sydney Olympic Games.  But don't
> worry, I have not approached the organising committee with any such
> suggestions.
>
>     But, for me, such do-gooding ideas are far in the future, considering
> how long it is taking just to make headway with the Language, Glosa, on its
> own.  I have no time to go off at tangents, at present.  Basically I am
> working hard at the political level to prevent Australia from suffering a
> fate, similar to the one that Alexander reports, as having befallen him in
> Russia.

I think you would accomplish much more with Glosa than politics.
Many people are working on the one, very few on the other.
Glosa now is threatened with extinction as a credible auxlang.

Local politics are irrelevant, only global economics matters.
Building a networked community that is international but
non-corporate and non-governmental, would be a possible solution.
The politics of opposition only result in deadlock or worse.

We need clever technological solutions that bypass the
politicians. We need to be active and do the work ourselves.
So, I say we should organize the world's first (or best)
multilingual auxlang vocabulary according to semantic fields.

A simple list cannot be learned at all by 99% of people, and a
fat fake dictionary like Gode's Interlingua cannot even be read.
Look at LFN's scheme and imagine how that could be improved:
http://www.ship.edu/~cgboeree/lfnvocab.html

I have made experimental web pages with forms with pull-down menus,
one for each semantic field. This could become a 1-page active
reference-thing in a way similar to that of Ogden's Basic English,
but containing thousands rather than hundreds of items.

It requires some time and energy and creativity and enthusiasm.
Or else, just let Glosa die with its authors. Sometimes a bad idea
blocks the path of good, like Esperanto did to better auxlangs.
If the few remaining Glosa leaders won't lead, followers must look
elsewhere. The idea of morphologically simple creolistic grammar
is good, but a senile/moribund Glosa can only block progress.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] exhaustion, apathy
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Tue, 18 Jul 2000 11:53:44 -0700


> >I made the Ido-Glosa comparison, to demonstrate/discover
> >that their basic roots are quite similar.

> *  Yes they must be similar if they have common source languages.

Esperanto does *not* look similar, Ido does because much work
was done to make the roots naturalistic. Glosa departed from
recognizable roots in the direction of phonetic spelling
and easier pronunciation for non-Europeans. So I expected
far less similarity than I found.

>     Sorting through dictionaries to find "commonality" was something that
> Ron Clark was doing - selecting Glosa vocabulary - in 1977, when I met him.
>     So, long prior to my joining discussion on the Net, I had already
> assumed that the sorting work had all been done.

The dictionary needs some work. Who will do it?
The language needs some leaders. Who will lead?
Today it is easier to organize vocabulary material,
and to communicate internationally and quickly.
Here is the ideal project to build the community
of users and new leaders.

>     In my introduction to Glosa, I was told that it was a language for
> science, and I still feel this to be fundamentally so.

That is a false and hopeless direction.
Scientists use Greek roots non-systematically,
simply to generate near-meaningless random names.
The only true language of science is mathematics.
Greek is not useful for an auxlang, Latin is best;
only the Latinized Greek bits are any good.
Hogben was wrong on this point, and times changed.

> though both words are in _Glosa 60000_, I still see
> the scientific name as the prime word

Nobody will ever say "lepidoptera" in real life.
Too long, too formal, too silly. But I now suggest that
instead of removing any words, better ones be added.
After a fair sample of actually-used Glosa writings
from at least 100 users are accumulated, *then* notice
which terms are most popular and mark them in BOLD TYPE.
Let the ugly words die of disuse. Let people generate
lots of Glosa dictionaries. Make a controversy, see
if anyone *cares*, let evolution decide the winners.

> *   However, to do this for every Glosa word would, I suspect, be
> unnecessary, sometimes misleading, and possibly restrictive.

I have changed my opinion on restrictiveness. Inclusiveness
is much better. Let a thousand flowers bloom. Free Glosa Now!

>     Where homonyms occur, in either Glosa or English, I would see such
> function/category statements as useful,  EG
>        bear (to hold) -        tena
>             (to give birth) -  nati
>             (animal)  -        ursus

English bare/bear/born/etc is unsatisfactory.
By cutting Glosa words too short, similar problems arise.
But long words like lepidopteras won't fly either.

> For the moment, I have no time or energy for the suggested compilations
> Charles suggests

A community of users can solve all these problems in practice.
The prophet finds the promised land, but the people must work it.
(Moses has just died, now where is Joshua?)

Hey Jay, I wish I could tell you more about it, but my deaf cousin Anne (who has
some limited auditory capacity yet prefers to use signing for direct
participation in the Deaf community, which she identifies with primarily in
spite of her option to "pass" as an " moderately impaired normal," and more
power to her!) is a natural at learning languages, but they are all sign
languages (A-ha! - what's the neurolgy behind _that_, I wonder? - same
as/different from mental processes of gifted spoken or written language
prodigies?) has told me about an "Interlingua" of signed languages she is
evangelizing for, that is based (like Interlingua) on signs and gestural syntax
patterns common to the largest Deaf communities globally (insofar as it is
possible) and named - at last! - its name at last! - "Gestuno" - a clever
coinage, si? - but that's as much as I know about it.  It seems to be fairly
contemporary in the sense that it has inspired a "cult" of sorts, that has
access to books with the signs the hybrid system uses, which supports a network
of practicioners in the Americas (at least) who maintain written contacts with
one another (online and intermittent newsletters,) as of last summer in any
case, when I learned of it from her.  Maybe further info can be gleaned by a
search engine poke at the embers ... I would have followed up on it myself, but
as usual my plate is 'way too full!  Happy hunting, if you are so inclined, and
until next time, keep the faith with your Interlingua site and email list! -
With best regards, Joseph Carson

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Michael Talbot-Wilson <mtw{at}birdseye{dot}view{dot}net{dot}au>
Date: Thu, 20 Jul 2000 22:12:42 +0930 (CST)

Glosa claims to have no inflexions.  Isn't 'ge-' a past participle
prefix as in German?  Isn't such a prefix an inflexion?

Middle English had this inflexion (actually y- or i-, e.g. "Sumer
is i-cumen in", where there is a past participle prefix and a past
participle suffix as in "gekommen").

Since all germanic languages except the notoriously primitive
modern German have abandoned it it's queer to see it return in
an IAL.  Why is such a strangely reactionary inflexion
needed?  Is there an alternative, i.e. can it be dispensed with?

Regards,
Michael Talbot-Wilson

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: "Marcel Springer" <m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de>
Date: Thu, 20 Jul 2000 21:26:33 +0200

Michael Talbot-Wilson pa grafo 00-07-20:
 > Glosa claims to have no inflexions.  Isn't 'ge-' a past participle
 > prefix as in German?  Isn't such a prefix an inflexion? ...
 > Why is such a strangely reactionary inflexion
 > needed?  Is there an alternative, i.e. can it be dispensed with?

Look at "plu".  It says, the following word is plural.  If we would
write it with an hyphen ("plu-feli" instead of "plu feli"), then we
have another prefix/inflexion.  You do not want "ge-"?  Take "ge "!

The "no-inflexions-no-grammar"-purism is important to those who want
mainly a curiosity ("Oh, a language without inflexions. How
bizarre!").  Glosa's destination must be higher.


 > ... the notoriously primitive modern German ...

Glosalist.

From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>

>  > ... the notoriously primitive modern German ...

> Eines Kommentars enthalte ich mich. Er verfehle das Thema der
> Glosalist.

Anyway, primitive is *good* for a 2nd language!

Imagine an auxlang based on Germanic roots rather
than the notoriously boring Greek and Latin.
English/Afrikaans syntax, sorta like Glosa.

German was too innovative in the area of sound changes
from the earlier common Germanic. Let's reverse the English
sound changes (i.e. use instead some kinda Scottish),
and use the spelling system of the 1600s.

All Nouns must be capitalized, and the final "silent" -e
Grapheme must be pronounced as it was before, as a Schwa.
We should also reinstate the Saxon's Plural since it fits
so well into the adj-noun Order of Things.

Since the Vowels do not mean much of Anything, as in
Gode's Interlingua, we will have no Need to terminate
every Word with a silly romantic Vowel.

As for modern Terminology, we simply adopt all
such Terms as they are used today in Iceland.
As for its Name, it should give an Impression
of reformed hypermodern retro-English.

Sounds like a Winner!

You misunderstand me.  I didn't say it was bad, but that it hadn't
evolved.  Like Icelandic (which presumably also retains the past
participle prefix) it has avoided the evolution that has
simplified the grammar of English, Dutch and the Skandinavian
languages.

It has cases/declensions, conjugations, grammatical gender and
other trappings of an ancient language.  Such languages, because
of their grammatical complexity, are difficult to learn.  Charles
is obviously joking when he says primitive is good in a 2nd
language.

Still, I didn't mean 'primitive' in a pejorative sense.  I was
asking why Glosa or any modern IAL would revive a particle which
disappeared 600 years ago from the inventors' language.

	Til seint Iame beo i-souht   that I schal a-signe,
	And no man go to Galys  bote he go for euere;
	And alle Rome-renners   for robbeours of bi-zonde
	Bere no seluer ouer see   that bereth signe of the kyng,
        Nouther grotes ne gold i-graue   with the kynges coroune,
	Vppon forfet of that fe   hose hit fynde at Douere,
	Bote hit beo marchaund othur his men   or messager with

	Or prouisours or preestes   that popes a-vaunset.

as distasteful as smileys :(

You correctly identified my attempt at humor, but I was also
refering to the difficulty most adults experience learning a language.
So I hijack the word "primitive" to mean "cleverly simple",
as in Lingua Franca or Chinook Jargon or etc.

Glosa has adopted a creole style of syntax, which Occidental's
author also saw as the syntax of the future (though he did not
think it acceptable to eurolang-speakers of his time).

Perhaps it would be better simply not to use them?

> Michael Talbot-Wilson wrote:

> Glosa has adopted a creole style of syntax, which Occidental's
> author also saw as the syntax of the future (though he did not
> think it acceptable to eurolang-speakers of his time).

That might be the beginning of a response to Alexander
E. Kirpichev, whose question remains unanswered: what is the
distinctive advantage of Glosa compared with other IALs, and why
is it worthwhile to learn it?

I too would be interested in a complete answer.  In "The Loom of
Language", Bodmer/Hogben claim that all planned languages up to
Novial (the latest, to them) are distinctly inferior to English as
an IAL, but that one distinctly better than English is possible,
and, it is hinted, is coming.

On the first point, their arguments and language, when discussing
particular IALs, do not inspire enormous confidence in their
intellectual integrity.  Or maybe I should just say, the debunking
sometimes misses its mark or is thin and dubious, and cumulatively
the treatment makes one suspect that a covert advocacy is going
on.

On the second, Interglossa is a failed language, abandoned by
Hogben.  This suggests that it was not a decisive improvement over
its predecessors.  Was it, or is Glosa, such at distinct
improvement as to make good the second part of the claim?  If so,
how?

Regards,
Michael Talbot-Wilson

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Fri, 21 Jul 2000 11:18:29 -0700

[snipped majorly]

> what is the distinctive advantage of Glosa compared with other IALs,
> and why is it worthwhile to learn it?

I have tried them all. They are all too hard to learn but one,
and that one may vary for each person. Language is inherently
and inescapably *very* difficult. Even one's native language
in which a lifetime of practice is invested, even that language
fails to express what we really mean, and is not clearly
understood even when crafted by the best speakers and writers.

Many of the best minds have attacked the problem of designing
a better vehicle for thought and international communication.
Descartes, Leibniz, Plato, Lao-Tse(!), Jesperson, Saussure;
philosophers, logicians, mathematicians, linguists.
They cast a small light among giant shadows ...

But we could all benefit greatly from a practical auxlang
right now. This is the critical moment in history, and we
are divided (and conquered, i.e. politically controlled)
in uselessly competing/conflicting ethnic/language factions
just at the point where global cooperation and understanding
is most needed. Small world, huge danger!

English isn't working to solve this problem of division,
there are too many barriers, in its archaic spelling that
not even its native children can master, its widely variant
pronunciation non-standards, its heavy reliance on idioms,
its myriad subtleties of word connotations.

A few auxlang ideas seem to work well. My own favorites are
Ido, Glosa, and Occidental, but none is best in all respects.
Boiled down to a few technical points:

* light part-of-speech marking
* small but powerful set of roots
* subject-verb-object phrase order
* consistent affixes/compounds
* mostly CV, no consonant clusters
* recognizable mnemonic vocabulary
* few rules and no exceptions

I've been able to successfully read/write/speak only Occidental.
It can be understood by the Novial/Ido/Interlingua people,
so I had hoped for unification there ... But minor details
often separate more effectively than major ones, it seems,
because mankind is unkind, quarrelsome and short-sighted.

> In "The Loom of Language", Bodmer/Hogben claim

> their arguments and language, when discussing
> particular IALs, do not inspire enormous confidence

Also, Mario Pei's "One Language for the World" was a major
influence on me. I agree that their arguments were leaky.
But they certainly inspired a lot of people (I think) in the
right direction. Today we understand more about how people
learn language, and it is clear that pidgin/creole languages
*are* the easiest for adults to learn, and that they are
sufficient in expressive/communicative power/efficiency.

Most people hate grammar, with a passion. Hogben and his
anointed successors have produced the least grammar-bound of
auxlangs. Glosa really should win therefore, unless it were
cluttered with unneeded frills and sloppy vocabulary, and was
not published in the one obvious place and manner (the WWW).

If people began to use such an auxlang in an actual ongoing
global speech community via the internet, a rapid convergence
would occur to purify the language beyond any individual's
ability to design it. Snowball on hill or in hell?

> Interglossa is a failed language, abandoned by Hogben.
> This suggests that it was not a decisive improvement

Lingua Franca *did* work for approximately a millenium.
I think the key is to discover the language, not create it.
Research into creoles, and the interlinguistics experiments
of the 1900's, all seem to point back to the LF approach.

I think Glosa is dying, despite so much interest and potential;
then a new project along these same lines might succeed.
Or else wait for machine translation, or use Occidental now.
I would like to see Wendy relinquish control of Glosa now to
someone more dynamic and visionary, who would open-source it;
maybe this list-owner, for instance. Glosa needs a version 3.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Patrick Dunn <tb0pwd1{at}corn{dot}cso{dot}niu{dot}edu>
Date: Fri, 21 Jul 2000 16:59:12 -0500 (CDT)

On Fri, 21 Jul 2000, Michael Talbot-Wilson wrote:

> You misunderstand me.  I didn't say it was bad, but that it hadn't
> evolved.  Like Icelandic (which presumably also retains the past
> participle prefix) it has avoided the evolution that has
> simplified the grammar of English, Dutch and the Skandinavian
> languages.

Languages don't evolve; they change.  This change is neither an
improvement nor a debasement.

I suspect that the ge- prefix (and I agree that it's a prefix; nonsense to
say a language without grammar -- it's like saying "a skeleton without
bones") comes from gene, the verb most often used in passive
constructions.  This is easy enough to remember.

> It has cases/declensions, conjugations, grammatical gender and
> other trappings of an ancient language.  Such languages, because
> of their grammatical complexity, are difficult to learn.  Charles
> is obviously joking when he says primitive is good in a 2nd
> language.

You mean an ancient language like Chinese, or Hawaiian, or . . . wait,
none of those have cases, conjugations, or other "trappings."

In truth, I doubt that a lack of declensions improves the learning curve
all that much.  For every noun case you absolish, you provide a
preposition or word order rule that must be learned.  We simply are more
used to showing case with prepositions than with declension -- so it seems
easier to us.  The very definition of ethnocentrism.

> Still, I didn't mean 'primitive' in a pejorative sense.  I was
> asking why Glosa or any modern IAL would revive a particle which
> disappeared 600 years ago from the inventors' language.

1.  Perhaps the ge- doesn't come from that particle, but from a
contraction of gene.
2.  It's easier to provide some particle to show participial relationships
than to make rules for circumlocution of participles.  The particle ge-
disappeared from most germanic languages because the participle was often
adequately marked alreay witha  suffix.


>
> 	Til seint Iame beo i-souht   that I schal a-signe,

history is an attractive one.  If Bruce Gilson is your "expert" of choice in

investigation?  Thanks for mentioning "WeDe" in any case; it will stimulate
follow-up elsewhere on this subject again at least, so til next time, With

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Michael Talbot-Wilson <mtw{at}birdseye{dot}view{dot}net{dot}au>
Date: Sat, 22 Jul 2000 13:54:41 +0930 (CST)

At 2000-07-21 16:59 -0500, Patrick Dunn wrote:

> On Fri, 21 Jul 2000, Michael Talbot-Wilson wrote:
> > [Something.]
>
> Languages don't evolve; they change.  This change is neither an
> improvement nor a debasement.

I quite agree that the changes that happen to a language over time
are totally devoid of moral significance.  I'm not absolutely
certain that they are without aesthetic significance, but it is
fruitless to discuss the point.  I'm not sure what else you might
mean by "improvement or debasement".  A language may be improved
in a particular pragmatic sense, e.g. improved for ease of
learning, by such changes.  I tried to make it clear that I did
not regard being primitive, or the reverse, as being either
improved or debased in any moral or comprehensive sense.

But philologists (I am told - don't mistake me for one) suffer
from the delusion that the changes that happen in a language over
time are not quite random, but on the whole move in particular
directions as time passes.  They think they have identified
characteristic changes which can be found in all languages.
Under the influence of this delusion they successfully predicted
the character of proto-Greek before Linear B had been deciphered.

It seems not totally unreasonable to refer to the changes that are
characteristically associated with the forward direction of time
as "evolution".

> > It has cases/declensions, conjugations, grammatical gender and
> > other trappings of an ancient language.  Such languages, because
> > of their grammatical complexity, are difficult to learn.  Charles
> > is obviously joking when he says primitive is good in a 2nd
> > language.
>
> You mean an ancient language like Chinese, or Hawaiian, or . . . wait,
> none of those have cases, conjugations, or other "trappings."

No.  I mean one that has not evolved as far as modern Chinese.
In fact ancient Chinese writings have complex grammar.

> In truth, I doubt that a lack of declensions improves the learning curve
> all that much.  For every noun case you absolish, you provide a
> preposition or word order rule that must be learned.  We simply are more
> used to showing case with prepositions than with declension -- so it seems
> easier to us.  The very definition of ethnocentrism.

I suggest that you read Bodmer's "The Loom of Language".  I don't
think you quite appreciate the reality.

Word order is not a problem in languages that have shed such
complexities.  It isn't something that has to be learned, it is
the only way you can do it.  Or to put it in another way, you can
order words in any way that works, although some ways may be more
popular with native speakers.  English, French, Ido and Glosa all
have a similar word order.  German is totally different, and in
classical Latin such a thing hardly exists.

> > 	Til seint Iame beo i-souht   that I schal a-signe,

While I'm waxing lyrical (I'll recover in a minute), probably the
best remembered and loved example of the y-, i- prefix is in
Chaucer's description of the Wife of Bath,

	Upon an amblere esily she sat,
	Ywympled wel, and on hir heed an hat

And now, as someone has already said (in a funny word order), this
is way off topic, and I'm bowing out.

Regards,
Michael Talbot-Wilson

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au

From: Patrick Dunn <tb0pwd1{at}corn{dot}cso{dot}niu{dot}edu>

On Sat, 22 Jul 2000, Michael Talbot-Wilson wrote:

> At 2000-07-21 16:59 -0500, Patrick Dunn wrote:

> > On Fri, 21 Jul 2000, Michael Talbot-Wilson wrote:
> > > [Something.]

> > Languages don't evolve; they change.  This change is neither an
> > improvement nor a debasement.
>
> I quite agree that the changes that happen to a language over time
> are totally devoid of moral significance.  I'm not absolutely
> certain that they are without aesthetic significance, but it is
> fruitless to discuss the point.  I'm not sure what else you might
> mean by "improvement or debasement".  A language may be improved
> in a particular pragmatic sense, e.g. improved for ease of
> learning, by such changes.  I tried to make it clear that I did
> not regard being primitive, or the reverse, as being either
> improved or debased in any moral or comprehensive sense.

In which case, what's your objection to the ge- again?

> But philologists (I am told - don't mistake me for one) suffer
> from the delusion that the changes that happen in a language over
> time are not quite random, but on the whole move in particular
> directions as time passes.  They think they have identified
> characteristic changes which can be found in all languages.
> Under the influence of this delusion they successfully predicted
> the character of proto-Greek before Linear B had been deciphered.

The changes in a language are not, by any means, random.  Do not, however,
take this fact and run with it to asume that there are, in fact,
characteristic changes "which can be found in all languages."  We were
able to determine the character of prot-Greek because we're quite familiar
with the processes of linguistic change in Indo-European languages -- but
apply the same set of sound changes that have affected, say, Greek, to,
say, German, and you'll end up with something incomprehensible to both.

> It seems not totally unreasonable to refer to the changes that are
> characteristically associated with the forward direction of time
> as "evolution".

Most people hold connotations of improvement in their definitions of
"evolution," but okay, assume not.  My point is, languages do not "evolve"
toward greater simplicity any more than they evolve toward greater
complexity.  They merely change.  After all, Old English was a *lot* more
regular than modern English -- just as Latin had more case endings than
Spanish.

> > > It has cases/declensions, conjugations, grammatical gender and
> > > other trappings of an ancient language.  Such languages, because

Let me also point out that Finnish is hardly an ancient language, and it
has all of the above.  You've got this strange linear view of history that
simply isn't tenable.  Most Indo-European languages have tended to drop
their declensions -- but that doesn't mean this is a universal through all
language groups.

> > > of their grammatical complexity, are difficult to learn.  Charles
> > > is obviously joking when he says primitive is good in a 2nd
> > > language.
> >
> > You mean an ancient language like Chinese, or Hawaiian, or . . . wait,
> > none of those have cases, conjugations, or other "trappings."
>
> No.  I mean one that has not evolved as far as modern Chinese.
> In fact ancient Chinese writings have complex grammar.

Again you have this sense of forward movement that *simply isn't there*.
Languages don't evolve "far" or "not as far."  They change.  Period.

> > In truth, I doubt that a lack of declensions improves the learning curve
> > all that much.  For every noun case you absolish, you provide a
> > preposition or word order rule that must be learned.  We simply are more
> > used to showing case with prepositions than with declension -- so it seems
> > easier to us.  The very definition of ethnocentrism.
>
> I suggest that you read Bodmer's "The Loom of Language".  I don't
> think you quite appreciate the reality.

Pray share with me what reality that might be.  I'm three hours from a
good university library right now, and can't get the article.

> Word order is not a problem in languages that have shed such
> complexities.  It isn't something that has to be learned, it is
> the only way you can do it.  Or to put it in another way, you can
> order words in any way that works, although some ways may be more
> popular with native speakers.  English, French, Ido and Glosa all
> have a similar word order.  German is totally different, and in
> classical Latin such a thing hardly exists.

"it isn't something that has to be learned."  Surely you can see the plain
stupidity of that statement?  Chompskian linguistics aside (far aside,
please), it's obvious that *all language is arbitrary* and therefore *all
language must be learned.*  This includes word order, declensions,
orthography, phonology, and all the nicities of grammar and structure that
you find so "primative."  Glosa is easier than natural language not
because it lacks declensions, but because it is *completely regular*.
Therefore, learning one rule is good enough for every word.

Let's try an example.  In Latin, the genitive of a noun ending in -us is
-i (assuming of course it's masc. -- there's a class of neuter nouns in
-us whose rules sometimes differ).  In Glosa, the genitive of a noun is
formed by prefixing the preposition [de].  Now, if all Latin nouns ended
in -us, and all Latin nouns formed their genitives by adding -i, then why
would that be inherently any harder to remember than the preposition "de"?
It simply would not.

The only reason you find languages with decleensions "harder" is the fact
that they are foreign to you.  This is ethnocentrism.  And the fact that
you consider languages with characteristics of your native language to be
more "evolved" than other languages is pure chauvinism.

>
> > > 	Til seint Iame beo i-souht   that I schal a-signe,
>
> While I'm waxing lyrical (I'll recover in a minute), probably the
> best remembered and loved example of the y-, i- prefix is in
> Chaucer's description of the Wife of Bath,
>
> 	Upon an amblere esily she sat,
> 	Ywympled wel, and on hir heed an hat
>
> And now, as someone has already said (in a funny word order), this
> is way off topic, and I'm bowing out.
>
> Regards,
> Michael Talbot-Wilson
>

Subject: Re: [glosalist] test - fingertalk (for deaf) letter "g" part of
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Sun, 23 Jul 2000 00:20:07 +1000

At 01:21 19/07/2000 -0700, Joseph Carson wrote:

>Hey Jay, I wish I could tell you more about it, but my deaf cousin Anne   ..... is a natural at learning languages, but they are all sign

>languages (A-ha! - what's the neurolgy behind _that_, I wonder? - same

>as/different from mental processes of gifted spoken or written language

>prodigies?) has told me about an "Interlingua" of signed languages she is

>evangelizing for, that is based (like Interlingua) on signs and gestural >syntax patterns common to the largest Deaf communities globally and named - at last! - its name at last! - "Gestuno" -

>    .....

>Jay Bowks wrote: "Not that this has anything to do with Glosa, but... it

>is interesting to note that there is no international manual alphabet of >sorts. que

>no?"

>

*   I have had time for this concept to run through the synapses.


    There definitely could be something in it for all of us.


    Sign Language has progressed far beyond the simplicity of spelling on your fingers, and could in fact be the most suitable a-cultural medium for communication across the cultures.


    In a similar vein is the new Langenscheidt's PICTURE TALK - Global Communication at Your Fingertips.  This is a small, pocket-book for the traveller who passes through many lands: the book has no words, and not even any page-numbers - because these would imply a cultural bias; instead, it has pictures grouped wiith things and situations you might wish to communicateover, during your travels: the communication is achieved by fingering the relevant picture and making the right associated gesture, pointing, questioning, angst, beckoning, etc.


   It's all going non-verbal and non-literal, folks!


   Signed communication can never reach the speed of reading, but would be comparable with the speed of speaking; and using the "Picture Talk" as a prop for communication might be a shade slower, again, but would get you out of tricky situation, with virtually no learning curve, and no need for specialised linguistic ability.


   Where does Glosa fit into this scenario?  The learning of a 2000 sign vocabulary could be comparable with getting the Glosa basic vocabulary under your belt, and the learning curve for using Glosa's syntax might be comparable with doing the same thing for signing's sytax and inflection.  However both Glosa and signing require the two people, in the communication pair, to be reasonably fluent in the communication system.  With Glosa, one can make up and deliver sentences, while checking for the odd unknown word from the dictionary .. similar to hunting for the right pictures in Picture Talk.

   And, of course Glosa has the advantage over signing, that a person can communicate across space by using a telephone, and across time with by using written notes.


   Perhaps in some future time the seasoned traveller will learn Glosa (or whatever the IAL is) and signing, and use one or the other dependiing on the skills of the person with whom he wishes to communicate; but also, he would carry a picture book to allow him to communicate with people who had neither literacy or signing.


Saluta,

  Robin

[this part is gratuitously ironic, please skip to next]

> In a similar vein is the new Langenscheidt's PICTURE TALK

> It's all going non-verbal and non-literal, folks!

I just started reading Joseph Conrad's story "Typhoon".
Maybe I should wait until it comes out as a picture-book.
Destroy the intercultural gap by destroying all culture?



[this is the meaty part, please consider it seriously]

> The learning of a 2000 sign vocabulary

I have never seen an auxlang basic vocabulary with
less than a 1300 or more than 5000 roots.
Gode's dictionary of 27000 words has around 5000 roots,
same as the Occidental Radicarium or Novial Lexike.
Adam Kilgariff's lemma-list for 93% English coverage
has 6000 items of which not all are roots.

It should be possible to merge a few of these lists
and have a sure-fire effective and practical vocabulary.
The equivalent roots can be added for a number of auxlangs,
particularly Glosa, which most desperately needs a
systematic full vocab. All synonyms should be included.

Then out of the full list of 6000, it should be apparent
that some roots are far more essential than others.
They could be scored by frequency of usage and stratified
into "top 1000", "basic 2500", and "full 6000".
In basic lists the less-favored synonyms can be dropped.

This would be an achievable and valuable project,
and could be shared among several auxlangs, I imagine.
It is a killing task for 1 person, but if several people
would work together it can be done in a short time.
Anybody here interested? Any ideas for improvements?
