To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] dictionary, was Re: fingertalk (for deaf)
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sat, 22 Jul 2000 11:46:53 -0700


> It should be possible to merge a few of these lists
> and have a sure-fire effective and practical vocabulary.
> The equivalent roots can be added for a number of auxlangs,
> particularly Glosa, which most desperately needs a
> systematic full vocab. All synonyms should be included.

Glosa roots (= words, usually) tend to a CVCV pattern
with the final vowel being meaningless 99% of the time.
Lingua Franca also had meaningless Romance euphonious
or etymological final vowels that were insignificant.
Ido roots are easily obtained by removing their final
part-of-speech vowel or verbal -ar suffixes.

So as an example, the English word "basket" is 3983th
in frequency in the Kilgariff list. Even if Glosa had
no previously defined word for it, one could easily be
found in LF or Ido, but actually Glosa has 2 of them.
So, I would like to have a table of Kilgariff roots
crossed with auxlangs sorta like so:

Kilga   LF     Glosa        Ido   Novial  Occid  Latin
basket  gorba  korba,mirso  korb  korb    corb   corbis

There might be a fair number of roots present in Glosa that
do *not* appear in K's list. Of course they would all be added
as well. What I am proposing is like Rick Harrison's list,
http://www.geocities.com/Athens/5383/uld2in.html
which should also be merged into this root table/database.
I think his other list is entirely subsumed by K's,
but might help define the "basic" vocab subset.
http://rick.harrison.net/langlab/vitaleng.html

Having such a list available on the web would *not* violate
anyone's copyright etc. It *might* lead to a convergence.
At least it would be interesting, and *useful* in filling
the gaps in our favorite auxlangs or conlangs.

> from a bull's foot on the subject.  You are totally ignorant.  At

Yet every current scholar on the subject (at least, every current
reputable scholar on the subject -- that weirdo who thinks Basque is made
of English anagrams might think otherwise) holds that languages do not
change in any universal direction.  They do not become more isolated, or
more inflected, over time.  In fact, inflected languages tend to become
isolated languages, and isolated languages tend to become agglutinative
and then inflected.

Your argument against the Glosa ge- is unfounded, absurd, and completely
ridiculous.  You hve not addressed any point at all which I have raised.
My perfectly good argument that inflected languages are not harder to
learn, inherently, than isolating languages was ignored -- insted you told
me to read a book which, upon examination, consists of amateur linguistic
sketches (with numerous errors) and a typically mid-century attitude long
since abandoned by every serious scholar in the field of linguistics.

There are plenty of perfectly good objections to Glosa (for instance, I
personally think that  alanguage without ambiguity is a language without
poetry, and if a language lacks poetry -- it is useless [of course, having
translated a tiny bit of poetry into Glosa, I must say the language *does*
contain ambiguity, despite assertions to the otherwise]).  But the
objection to the ge- prefix on the grounds that it's unnecessary (yes,
technically, any one feature of language can be circumlocuted -- skimmed
an interesting book on the topic once, reducing language to a set of fifty
[I think] primatives) lacks merit.  The use of ge- allows the flexibility
of the passive voice, a technique that permits the poetic ambiguity above
mentioned.  Your further argument that ge- comes from German and is
therefore bad is completely baffling.  First, I see no evidence other than
coincidence of form that ge- comes from the similar Germanic preffix.
Second, I see no reason why adopting, even if it did come from such an
origin, a handy participial prefix is bad.  Your attitude puzzles me, your
arguments baffle me, and your sources tickle me.

Incidently, it is only "pretense" if I do not, in fact, have the education
I imply.  Since I have a MA and am working on my PhD, I don't think it's
technically pretense.  Hauteur, maybe, pretense, no.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sun, 23 Jul 2000 01:19:30 -0700


> And, What do you think about Esperanto, the most popular conlang in the
> world?

Ido made the 2 most necessary improvements to Esperanto,
eliminating the goofy circumflexed consonants, and using
more naturalistic-looking roots. Here is a great URL:

http://geocities.com/Athens/Forum/5037/

Esperanto is not so popular as its propaganda claims.
Nearly everybody tries to learn it, fails and quits,
losing faith in the idea of an IAL. Very bad result!
All other IALs are superior technically to Esperanto.
But I like very much the idealism of Zamenhoff, and
the nice sense of community that Esperantists promote.

Occidental is even much better than Ido, because
it uses more naturalistic affixes as well as roots.
De Wahl proved that Ido is not so "scientific" as it claims,
and replaced its false-logic with a truer psychological logic.
The thing that works best for me is the analytic verb
phrase structure in Occidental, modeled on Lingua Franca.

Today it may be possible to improve IAL design by study
of creolistics. Perhaps now a more radical design would
be acceptable to western Europeans and Americans.

Glosa has the right basic syntax, again like Lingua Franca;
I believe that "analytical" is the right way to go for a
global auxlang, though no disrespect is intended toward
"synthetic" Russian and other great cultural languages,
they just don't serve well as auxlangs (nor does English).

You are irrelevantly extending the universe of discussion.  I am
very well aware of this fact.  (I found it out from "The Loom of
Language", which discusses the discovery of it in Indian
languages.)  But what might happen afterwards, to English or
Chinese, is irrelevant to the fact of the retention of an antique
inflexion in Glosa, one which provably is unnecessary because most
Germanic languages have dropped it.

You have now, though, accidentally, admitted that there is
directed evolution, the fact you have been trying to deny.  Why
have you created an argument on the point, when the most you can
now say is that the evolution is cyclic?

> me to read a book which, upon examination, consists of amateur linguistic
> sketches (with numerous errors) and a typically mid-century attitude long
> since abandoned by every serious scholar in the field of linguistics.

I can trust you to be right on that, of course?  What would you
know about serious scholars in the field of linguistics?

You insinuate, without clearly saying, that you have examined the
book I mentioned.  "Upon examination"?  Whose?  You still need to
read it.

"Perfectly good"??  The issue is in part the number of forms of
the noun, or the verb, that have to be memorised.  I am not going
to quote extensively.  Read it.  Read about the number of forms
for the finite verb in Sanscrit, Greek, Latin and English.  The
numbers drop from staggeringly large to insignificant.  The
problem with inflexions is first that you have to memorise them,
and second that the systems of classification invented by
grammarians, such as some small number of declensions or
conjugations, is artificial.  The language in the wild is much
less regular.  If Bodmer is right, no-one for centuries has
studied classical Latin.  At least at school, in non-specialist
courses.  What is studied is an artificial simplification.  The
real language did not have the regularity of the noun and verb and
adjective classifications that are taught.  Overwhelmingly, in
Latin, and even more so in ancient Greek and Sanscrit a set of
case endings is unique to a particular noun, and correspondingly
for verbs.

Your perfectly good argument that inflected languages are not more
difficult to learn, Haw haw.

> There are plenty of perfectly good objections to Glosa (for instance, I

> Incidently, it is only "pretense" if I do not, in fact, have the education
> I imply.  Since I have a MA and am working on my PhD, I don't think it's

In what discipline, pray?  Still disclosing nothing unless it is
prised out of you?

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] dictionary, was Re: fingertalk (for deaf)
From: "Marcel Springer" <m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de>
Date: Sun, 23 Jul 2000 22:52:19 +0200

Karo Charles,

 > I think Glosa is dying, despite so much interest and potential;

Tu plu grama habe alo tem spe alo tem no-spe pro Glosa.  Place ne
lose koragi!

Your mails are swinging between hope for Glosa and desparation.
Please do not give up!

 > They could be scored by frequency of usage and stratified
 > into "top 1000", "basic 2500", and "full 6000".
 > In basic lists the less-favored synonyms can be dropped.

The internet dictionary project of W. Patterson/ R. Gaskell/ P.
Bartlett (www.neosplice.com/~ailanto/glosa.htm) goes in that
direction.  Let us improve this one before starting another
project.

 > So, I would like to have a table of Kilgariff roots
 > crossed with auxlangs sorta like so:
 >
 > Kilga   LF     Glosa        Ido   Novial  Occid  Latin
 > basket  gorba  korba,mirso  korb  korb    corb   corbis

This may be helpful.  My suggestion for strategy:

You certainly know Interlingua de Peano/ Latino sine flexione
(see www.geocities.com/Athens/Olympus/2948/).  It is a direct
forerunner of Glosa and nearly the same as Glosa.  Main difference:
Glosa has a limited (= good) but unsatisfying vocabulary.  Interlingua
has no own vocabulary.  It uses the Latin one and a simple word
derivation scheme instead.  With this scheme, - a bit refined -, we
could improve Glosa words.

Examples:
  Glosa words: "silva" and "silvi" for forest, wood
  Latin:       silva, ae, f., ablativus silva, then ...:
  Peano:       silva
  --->         "silva" is good, "silvi" is bad and should be
                 eliminated.
(I started to make a list of those bad synonyms, will post them later
for discussion.)

If Peano leads to a bad result (for example "flexione" has too many
syllables), prefer the shortest old Glosa word or the best solution of
other IALs.

 > Glosa roots (= words, usually) tend to a CVCV pattern
 > with the final vowel being meaningless 99% of the time.

I agree.  This is very important!  But do not kill all of them.
"silv" would be harder to speak (consonant cluster with the following
word), harder to understand by listening and sounds uglier.

Saluta

Marcel (m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de, www.tu-harburg.de/~smms1406/glosa.htm)

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] dictionary, was Re: fingertalk (for deaf)
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sun, 23 Jul 2000 15:01:40 -0700

Marcel Springer wrote:

> The internet dictionary project of W. Patterson/ R. Gaskell/ P.
> Bartlett (www.neosplice.com/~ailanto/glosa.htm) goes in that
> direction.  Let us improve this one before starting another
> project.

As long as we do *something* about it, anything, I will be happy.
There hasn't been any effort since Robin's latest M$ crash.
It is impossible for any person to do alone, but ideal for
a community-effort project to resusitate Glosa.

> You certainly know Interlingua de Peano/ Latino sine flexione
> (see www.geocities.com/Athens/Olympus/2948/).  It is a direct
> forerunner of Glosa and nearly the same as Glosa.  Main difference:
> Glosa has a limited (= good) but unsatisfying vocabulary.  Interlingua
> has no own vocabulary.  It uses the Latin one and a simple word
> derivation scheme instead.  With this scheme, - a bit refined -, we
> could improve Glosa words.

This is a great idea! I never thought of LsF as similar to Glosa.
It does come to a similar solution, from the opposite direction ...

>   Glosa words: "silva" and "silvi" for forest, wood
>   Latin:       silva, ae, f., ablativus silva, then ...:
>   Peano:       silva
>   --->         "silva" is good, "silvi" is bad and should be
>                  eliminated.
> (I started to make a list of those bad synonyms, will post them later
> for discussion.)

Cleanup of errant/variant final vowels should be done, and clearly
you do have the ability to accomplish that. Then we would have
a clean Glosa 6000 to analyze further (as planned a year or 2 ago).

>  > Glosa roots (= words, usually) tend to a CVCV pattern
>  > with the final vowel being meaningless 99% of the time.
>
> I agree.  This is very important!  But do not kill all of them.
> "silv" would be harder to speak (consonant cluster with the following
> word), harder to understand by listening and sounds uglier.

I think a bare root really is CVC (silv-) but the word *does* need
a euphonious and etymologically plausible vowel final (silvA).
Most of the world strongly prefers or requires CVCV words.

What I much *dislike* is the use of final vowels to differentiate
meanings, e.g. "bale=ballet, bali=throw". That's a big mistake,
fortunately there are not very many of those in the current vocab.
If one forgets the correct final, which is so very easy to do,
it should *not* matter. Since the finals are unstressed, nobody can
hear the difference between "bale/bali" in actual speech anyway.
Besides, I hate ballet ... and to me "baila=dance" (from Spanish).

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sun, 23 Jul 2000 19:29:29 -0700

Patrick Dunn wrote:

> In any event, I suspect that ge- cannot be circumvented; a participle
> saves massive amounts of time and adds a certain simplicity to the
> language that otherwise would be bled away in circumlocutions and
> ambiguities.

It's a very interesting (troublesome) problem.
Some examples from the well-known web page:

        ge-lose feli               Lost cat
        Id frakti; id pa frakti.   It is breaking; it broke.
        Id pa gene frakti.         It got broken.
        Id es ge-frakti.           It is broken.
        u feli; qi ...             The cat which ...

It looks like the only options would be "ge-" and "qi".
And there's no present participle BTW IINM.

One might be tempted to try "pa-lose feli" and "nu-frakti violina",
but they'd be tough to distinguish from real verb phrases in speech.
With regular words vowel-terminated, maybe -nt and -d, or -nd and -t?
Then, "loset feli" and "fractint violina". Nicer, but not Glosa.

Using a relative clause seems the worst and clumsiest solution,
and I might even prefer to use participles entirely,
replacing all relative clauses, with the side-benefit of
eliminating the *ridiculous* "Semicolon de Hogben".

So maybe the thing to do is keep using "ge-", and justt
add a new particle for the present/active participle??
But they might get mixed up into verb phrases. Oy!!

I can picture that precisely, it is a common occurance:
http://catty.com/~catty/jpg/kt_keybd.jpg

Another temptation is to use the Saxon Genitive, as in
"the cat's writing was incisive". It seems strange that
Hogben so greatly praised Chinese grammar but selected
the euro-genitive preposition "de" instead of the sino-
postposition "de", which is so much more useful,
serving for both relative clauses and genitives.

From: "Marcel Springer" <m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de>

Charles pa grafo:
 > So maybe the thing to do is keep using "ge-", and justt
 > add a new particle for the present/active participle??

Interesting.  First Michael wanted to kill "ge-" and the past
participle.  Now we go into the contrarian direction and want to
create a new participle (present participle).  But the argument is
right.  I have the same problems, too.  Three notes from me:

- In German the present participle is normally not used.  It is bad
  style.  But it is available.
- In English the present participle is used more often.  But often it
  is only the sign of the continuous form.  Therefore Glosa has "du".
- My suggestion:
    Try to avoid it!  Else "u skribi feli" is "the writing cat", if
    there is no ambiguity.  Else make a new full sentence "u feli
    skribi e ... " (coordination) or take a prepositional clause like
    "u feli ko skribi akti".

Saluta

Marcel (m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de, www.tu-harburg.de/~smms1406/glosa.htm)

>I usually don't post here because I have let my Glosa get so rusty it's

>dictionary.  But I have to ask: since when do we judge languages are

>"primative?"  Primative by what standard?

>

>This is obvious ethnocentricism.  Makes me sad.

>

Karo Plu Amika,

  Patrick, which is the ethnocentricism?

  The use of  ~ge-~  in Glosa, or references to the German language?

  On general principles I would find reference to national languages distracting, in Glosa,  however, on occasion, a natlang reference is pertinent.  Ron Clark, originally had imagined that Glosa, like any good science report, would always use the Active Voice.  Subsequently he added the mechanism for creating thhe participle,, which is the mainstay of the Passiive Voice: so, the ~ge~ participle-former is a relative new-comer to Glosa.


  The concept of "primitiveness," for me, takes two forms in language:-

    a. if a language has many featues that are similar to those present when it was first recognised as a distinct language, then it is developmentally relatively primitive; and

    b. if a language has a low level of complexity, then it is organisationally primitive.

  In the case of Glosa, I hark back to the lexical roots, to suggest that, being based on the Classical roots of Greek and Latin, by comparison with the deriivative European languages, it is relatively primative.  As well as that, I would suggest that, although many ethnic languages do feature very complex tenses and inflectional structures, I understand that root-word languages, like Chinese and Glosa, are developmentally and structurally more primitive than are the more inflected languages.


   Good news: the _Glosa 2000_ dictionary is alive and well and available from Richmond, UK.  I bought one two months ago; it is still in new condiition, despite use, and could be sent to you.  Its 12Stg price-tag ammounted to a cost of approx A$20.  If you chose to acqquire mine, I'd sendiit to as soon as you sent your land address.  Then I would buy another from Wendy Ashby at Richmond.  I include here my signature in case you wished to write directly to Wendy, yourself.


Saluta,


Robn Gaskell

    Incidentally, using some sort of rich-text or other proprietary
mail format messes things up for some of us.

On Mon, 24 Jul 2000, Robin Gaskell wrote:

> [...]

>     Whenever I have said, that of the Planned Languages, Glosa is the
>            <bold>closest to the Language of Thought, </bold>I have been
> jumped on.  However, this highly intuitive observation remains the best I
> can do.

    Let me quote from a work of fiction.  It may not be the best
ficion around (nothing on the level of Tolkien), but it may serve
here to illustrate a point.  (I typed this up some time ago.)

=======

    Consider the following excerpt from David Eddings' fantasy novel,
"Domes of Fire" (1992).  Eddings' fantasy series "The Elenium" and its
sequel "The Tamuli" are set in a multi-racial, multi-lingual, medieval
world with the common motifs of chivalry, magic, religion, and so on.
Kalten is a brave and likable, but not always bright, Church Knight of
Elenic language, race, and religion, and the Church Knights are trained
to cast spells using the Styric tongue.  Sephrenia is a polyglot wisewoman
of the Styric race.  In this episode, an Elenic party are in the Tamul
Empire and need to learn to speak Tamul.  Incidentally, linguistic theory
is not my point here.  My point is the misjudgment of considering that
one's own language is somehow the "natural" way that all humans think.

        "Kalten, dear," [Sephrenia] said gently, why was I trying
      to teach you Styric?"
        "So that I could perform magic tricks, I guess."  He
      shrugged.  "That's unless you just enjoy making people
      suffer."
        "No, dear one.  It was just as painful for me as it was for
      you."  She shuddered.  "More painful, probably.  You _were_,
      in fact, trying to learn Styric so that you could work the
      spells, but in order to do that, you had to be able to _think_
      in Styric.  You can't just mouth the words and make them come
      off the way you want them to."
        "Wait a minute," he objected.  "Are you saying that people
      who speak other languages don't think the same way we do?"
        "They may think the same way, but they don't think in the
      same words."
        "Do you mean to say that we actually think in words?"
        "Of course we do.  What did you think thoughts were?"
        "I don't know.  But we're all human.  Wouldn't we all think
      in the same way and in the same language?"
        She blinked.  "And which language would that be, dear one?"
        "Elenic, naturally.  That's why foreigners aren't as clever
      as we are.  They have to stop and translate their thoughts from
      Elenic into that barbarian gabble they call language.  They do
      it just to be stubborn, of course."
        She stared at him suspiciously.  "You're actually serious,
      aren't you?"
        "Of course.  I thought everybody knew that's why Elenes are
      smarter than everybody else."  His face shone with blinding
      sincerity.
        "Oh, dear," she sighed in near despair,

=======

    Sorry, Robin, but I just can't agree that "of the Planned
Languages, Glosa is the closest to the Language of Thought."

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Mon, 24 Jul 2000 17:45:53 -0700


> *   In what sense do feel 'offended' by the use of the Glosa abbreviations
> that form the "generic" terms?
>     They represent abbreviated compounds, EG
>       pani boteka   [bread shop]
>       pani-boteka
>       pani-bo       [baker's]
>
>    Thhese generics make the languagebmuch more usable for me, and they slip
> off the tongue easily, as well.

That's just *morphology*, i.e. suffixes and prefixes
in a thin disquise, eliminating Glosa's claim to fame.
(I'm not counting word-compounding as morphology.)
There is no other well-known creole-like auxlang, that's
my interest in Glosa (perhaps Lingua Franca can be revived).

As a set of word-building affixes, the "bo-" set are clearly
inferior to those of the other auxlangs, Ido for instance.
They do not add anything to expressivity, but increase
complexity and the all-important difficulty of learning.
Far better to drop them and use plain compounds, I think.

If there must be morphology in Glosa, I would prefer
participles or some such useful things. But that would
be like a surrender of principles, not a good direction.

========
To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Solution to Glosa's Vocab problem
From: "Jay Bowks" <jjbowks{at}adam{dot}cheshire{dot}net>
Date: Sun, 25 Jul 1999 09:20:02 -0400

From: Patrick Dunn <tb0pwd1{at}corn{dot}cso{dot}niu{dot}edu>
>Now, if someone would just *tablulate* such rules, we could begin coining
>words left and right.

But part and parcel of Glosa is its word economy.
The original Interglossa idea was to use a minimum
set of roots as in Basic English but derive these from
Greek, or mainly from Greek and then assisted by Latin.
The fact that there are more Latin roots in Glosa is the
work of Ashby and Clark.

Eventually the dual feature of some roots, having both
Greek and Latin roots as synonyms, will catch up with
usage and a preferred root would be used more than the
other.

I do agree that 1000 words is just bare minimum. And
1200 to 2500 words would be more usable; also the
Glosa 6000 would need weeding out and more work
done on compounding for the sake of adding terms
to the wordlists instead.

Since,
Jay B.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Michael Talbot-Wilson <mtw{at}birdseye{dot}view{dot}net{dot}au>
Date: Tue, 25 Jul 2000 12:44:30 +0930 (CST)

Charles wrote:

>
> > *   In what sense do feel 'offended' by the use of the Glosa abbreviations
> > that form the "generic" terms?
> >     They represent abbreviated compounds, EG
> >       pani boteka   [bread shop]
> >       pani-boteka
> >       pani-bo       [baker's]
> >
> >    Thhese generics make the languagebmuch more usable for me, and they slip
> > off the tongue easily, as well.
>
> That's just *morphology*, i.e. suffixes and prefixes
> in a thin disquise, eliminating Glosa's claim to fame.
> (I'm not counting word-compounding as morphology.)
> There is no other well-known creole-like auxlang, that's
> my interest in Glosa (perhaps Lingua Franca can be revived).
>
> As a set of word-building affixes, the "bo-" set are clearly
> inferior to those of the other auxlangs, Ido for instance.
> They do not add anything to expressivity, but increase
> complexity and the all-important difficulty of learning.
> Far better to drop them and use plain compounds, I think.
>
> If there must be morphology in Glosa, I would prefer
> participles or some such useful things. But that would
> be like a surrender of principles, not a good direction.

This isn't an attempt to knock Glosa.  I'm just trying to clutch
at the spirit of it, grasp what it is all about, see if there is
something in there that is really the way forward.  It is a later
invention than Ido: is it based on a fundamentally better
approach, and does it show?

Is it, for example, easier to learn?  Are creoles and pigins
really easy to learn, or are they just an unsophisticated
compromise?  Do they work well, or do they just allow people to
get by after a fashion?  Are they just where you finish up when
improvising across a language barrier?  If so, does that fact mean
they are easy, or just minimal?  Is minimal good, or just limited?

Is Chinese really easy to learn? Issues relating to Chinese may
include (a) there are too many words; (b) grammatical
simplification was accompanied by great elaboration of idiomatic
phraseology.  Has such a set of helper phrases (presumably a few
thousand of them) been provided for Glosa?

On ease of learning of Glosa, the most conspicuous piece of
evidence that I have discovered is that the discussions in this
list are carried on in English and not Glosa.  If there were
simply a FAQ in English posted here regularly, and the actual
discussions took place in Glosa itself, I'd be more hopeful that
the language had something going for it.

Does it take 60 years to invent or instinctively create a pidgin
or creole?  Why, nearly 60 years after Hogben's publication, are
we doubtfully trying to work out how to speak of a writing cat, or
a writing person?  How many people use this language in real
communication, for example, internationally?  What meetings have
taken place in which the proceedings were entirely in Glosa?

One of Hogben's key insights, or so he thought, concerned the
importance of word economy, as exemplified by Basic English.  The
thing that takes so long in learning a language is acquiring the
words.  The only criticism he has of Peano's Interlingua was that
there were too many words.  It met his approval in having little
grammar.

It should therefore be possible to carry on written discussions in
Glosa using just the vocabulary of 1000 or so words that Robin or
someone else has already put up on the net and which I printed out
one time.  I'd like to see you experienced Glosa people doing
that.

I have tried as a rank beginner, with rather little success.  I
could only guess and hope that the accumulation of words was
intelligible.  It seems that because there is little formal
grammar there is no formal sentence.  There is nothing you can
analyse and determine to be a complete sentence, and therefore a
verifiably complete and verifiably intelligible communication of a
thought.  Whereas a rank beginner in Ido has no difficulty in
creating a sentence.

And that language is the normal medium of communication among
students of it.  And the root+suffix system is a reasonable
response to the word-economy issue.  That's why Zamenhof adopted
it in Esperanto.

On ease of use and ease of learning, Glosa does not even seem to
be in the game.  Therefore, what are we doing here?  Is it just a
matter of loyalty, of irrational attachment?

I've looked around on the Internet trying to find what is the key
advantage of Glosa relative to other IALs.  As I understand it,
the key claim is that it has no inflexions, genders, "or
diacritics" (!).  And that it has special words (rather than
inflexions) to signify the plural.  That is the key claim, and
presumably is supposed to be the key advantage.  Is it an
advantage?  We have seen (and we are seeing above) that Glosa has
proceded to acquire inflexions.  Interlingua also acquired
grammar.

Glosa seems to be a language that after nearly 60 years of
development, four times as long as any other IAL, has not reached
the point of being usable, and is not used.  Is this a fair
assessment, or am I being unreasonable, or clueless?

I can understand that it might be of interest to students of
language, of linguistics, as an object of experiment.  Is this the
real purpose of Glosa?

A toy for linguists I can understand and sympathise with.  Of
course, that would be a specialist tool that the rest of us would
be ill-advised to waste our time with.

Regards,
Michael Talbot-Wilson

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Mon, 24 Jul 2000 21:25:40 -0700

Michael Talbot-Wilson wrote:

Many interesting points ...

> Is it, for example, easier to learn?  Are creoles and pigins
> really easy to learn

Yes, especially for adults who are not linguistically talented.
Adult 2nd-language-learners are *the* market for any true IAL.
For over 800 years there was a perfectly acceptable one
in actual use all aroung the Mediterranean, and it probably
spawned most of the other creoles such as Tok Pisin.
It was the Lingua Franca, based on Occitan/Italian/Catalan,
with a structure very like ... Glosa.

> On ease of learning of Glosa, the most conspicuous piece of
> evidence that I have discovered is that the discussions in this
> list are carried on in English and not Glosa.

By using Latin synonyms and having a better dictionary,
people might write more in Glosa. I can do it easily enough
in Occidental, and without suffixes it gets even easier.
That does put a mighty heavy load on word-compounding ...

> Does it take 60 years to invent or instinctively create a pidgin
> or creole?  Why, nearly 60 years after Hogben's publication, are
> we doubtfully trying to work out how to speak of a writing cat,

Because I like griping a lot. With such a minimal grammar,
any mistakes really stand out ... I think Glosa is still
one major revision away from success. But it should work,
because the Lingua Franca did. There were only a few
years of design, during the 1940's and the 1970's.

> It should therefore be possible to carry on written discussions in
> Glosa using just the vocabulary of 1000 or so words

No way! 1000 is too few. Glosa has 1800 published on the web,
another 4000 in the "deluxe edition". Not all were well-chosen,
but if users felt free to expand the vocabulary, then in a
year or two (I think) it *would* rapidly evolve to usability.
The authors have choked their baby by holding it too tight
(and making it speak mangled Greek instead of vulgar Latin).

> It seems that because there is little formal
> grammar there is no formal sentence.

1. u/plu (ADJ) NOUN es/pa/nu/etc VERB u/plu (ADJ) NOUN

2. add prepositional phrases after nouns

That's enough! Adding more features just makes it worse.

Glosa es ma facili de Esperanto!!
Glosa nu debi repara u lexi-lista ...

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Michael Talbot-Wilson <mtw{at}birdseye{dot}view{dot}net{dot}au>
Date: Tue, 25 Jul 2000 14:59:32 +0930 (CST)

Charles wrote:

> For over 800 years there was a perfectly acceptable one
> in actual use all aroung the Mediterranean, and it probably
> spawned most of the other creoles such as Tok Pisin.
> It was the Lingua Franca, based on Occitan/Italian/Catalan,
> with a structure very like ... Glosa.

Yes, but I'm trying to grasp how effective Lingua Franca was,
whether a bare facilitator of cross-cultural communication, or a
flexible language which could facilate, for example, precise
explication of a theory.

I'm slightly doubtful that LF spawned Tok Pisin.

If my understanding is correct, LF has been lost and its detailed
structure is a matter of speculation.  It had a simpler grammar
than French (though it called itself the French language).  But
was it as grammatically simple as the ideal creole or pidgin?

There are other IALs with stuctures like those of Catalan and
Italian, greatly simplified.

Regards,
Michael Talbot-Wilson

back again automatically turns any classical Greek dictionary into a Glosa

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Tue, 25 Jul 2000 19:45:13 +1000

At 00:51 22/07/2000 +0930, Michael Talbot-Wilson wrote:
>At 2000-07-21 01:00 -0700, Charles wrote:
>
>> Michael Talbot-Wilson wrote:
>>
>> Glosa has adopted a creole style of syntax, which Occidental's
>> author also saw as the syntax of the future (though he did not
>> think it acceptable to eurolang-speakers of his time).
>
>That might be the beginning of a response to Alexander
>E. Kirpichev, whose question remains unanswered: what is the
>distinctive advantage of Glosa compared with other IALs, and why
>is it worthwhile to learn it?
>
>I too would be interested in a complete answer.  In "The Loom of
>Language", Bodmer/Hogben claim that all planned languages up to
>Novial (the latest, to them) are distinctly inferior to English as
>an IAL, but that one distinctly better than English is possible,
>and, it is hinted, is coming.
>
>On the first point, their arguments and language, when discussing
>particular IALs, do not inspire enormous confidence in their
>intellectual integrity.  Or maybe I should just say, the debunking
>sometimes misses its mark or is thin and dubious, and cumulatively
>the treatment makes one suspect that a covert advocacy is going
>on.
>
>On the second, Interglossa is a failed language, abandoned by
>Hogben.  This suggests that it was not a decisive improvement over
>its predecessors.  Was it, or is Glosa, such at distinct
>improvement as to make good the second part of the claim?  If so,
>how?
>
*   Phew!
    I will answer the two main questions in one piece.
    I do apologise to all, but with civilisation under threat, I have had
to 'de-prioritise' the Glosa work.

    The quick, and nasty, answer is that learning Glosa is not useful, at
present.  I have written three fairy stories into Glosa, and Wendy has
written one.  There is no body of Glosa literature, neither translations or
originals.  It is as if Glosa has got stuck at the "work in progress" stage.
    However, the nicer answer is that Glosa offers a promise of ready
communication .. especially at the technical level, but also, it can be
used with a distinctly human feel.  The 'promise' has not been taken up -
in a world hell-bent on conspicuous consumption and a self-destructive,
aggressive competition for what is left of the Planet's resources.  There
seems no time for people to contemplate ideas that are simply worthwhile,
but not immediately profitable.

    While I cannot speak for Bodmer and Hogben, nor for their 'scathing'
attitude towards other auxlangs, I can say, from my discussions with Ron
Clark, that he never published any critical comment about other designed
languages.

    The language Hogben hined at was obviously Interglossa; however, he
found that no-one was interested in his simple language in Post-War Europe,
so he stopped worrying about solving the problem of global scientific
communication, and the Cold War rift did not help, either.

    So the hard thing to say is that Glosa, which is even simpler,
morphologically, than Interglossa, has a very valuable part to play in our
'globalising' (with a little "G") world.
    The Internet cries out for a single 'international' communication
medium, and Glosa could rapidly become it.  Had Bill Gates acquired Glosa,
by now, all PCs would be carrying Glosa parsers in their operating systems.
 When it comes to man-machine intefaces, with meta-languages acting as the
interface media, Glosa would fit in perfectly.
    Maybe I should sell Glosa to Bill Gates: that would solve financial
problems for Wendy and me; and would cut the competioion dead, in the
distinctive Microsoft way!

     I have recieved letters fron Chinese people, saying Glosa's syntax was
easy for them, because it was about the same as that of Chinese: one sixth
of the problem solved!
     In my discussions with the Chinese Linguist, LIU Haitao, we used a
combination of Glosa and Esperanto - he writing Esperanto, and me Glosa -
and I have to go on record, as saying that I had to translate his Esperanto
into English before I could be sure I knew what he meant.  Haitao had used
Esperanto for a number of years, and could use Glosa quite happily, too;
however, he was lost when he ventured into English.  He did feel that Glosa
had been oversimplified, but I feel he did not give Glosa a fair trial ..
sticking with the Esperanto that he knew.
      Africans I have written to using Glosa, wrote back in good Glosa, and
did not quibble about this usage, or that: I sort of assume they felt the
language was fairly 'natural.'
      Writing scientific reports in Glosa is very dry and very logical:
perhaps Glosa could be described as "techno-speak."
      So, there we go: if the idea of a language being intuitive, and
logical, have any validity, then so has Glosa.  While designing the
'speak-as-you-look-it-up-in-the-dictionary' language, Ron Clark also had
the good sense to ensure that both the appearance and the sound of the
phrasing was pleasant, as well as making sense.
      Regarding the rules, and there is basically only one - a word is
modified by its precedent - Ron was horrified at the thought that learners
should sit down and internalise a grammar book of rules; children certainly
don't.  So Ron decreed that people would speak Glosa without first studying
a Grammar book.  In fact, he virtually banned the idea of producing such a
guide.  This was, perhaps his biggest mistake: he uvastly over-estimated
the initiative that could be shown by those making up the bulk of the human
race.  For research purposes, and against the wishes of Ron and Wendy, I
did set up a tabulation of Glosa's grammar, but this was purely
descriptive, and not prescriptive.
      While this tabulation of Glosa's grammar is in reasonable shape, I
would NOT recommend first 'learning the grammar' so that you could 'apply'
the rules when you spoke or wrote Glosa.  I would follow Ron in suggesting
that learners understood enough of the philosophy behind the language so
they could learn by 'jumping-in-the-deep-end' and
'speaking-as-they-looked-it-up-in-the-dictionary'.

      Sorry about the rambling answers.

     The matter of Glosa's possible superiority over its ccompetitors begs
a question.  It assumes that the present amateur promotions of Planned
Languages represents a valid Darwinian competition in which the 'the
survivor must be the fittest.'
     The evaluation of the situation requires more intelligent thinking:
which language, if adopted to serve as the world's IAL, will most benefit
the human race, this is the correct question.  And such an innovation
begins at Mother's knee, and is followed at the School M'am's desk: so, the
experiment, in which Klaus Lankilde taught Glosa to Middle-School children,
near Atlanta was a runaway success.

     And finally, I do feel that such questions are a waste of my time
answering.  There is a distinct three-strikes-and-you're-out sensation
about it: Lancelot and Ron have been struck out; now we are just waiting to
see how long I can keep batting for, until the innings is over.  We are,
however, still watching to see if Wendy Ashby is going to send in a curly
ball from outfield, to revive the game.

Saluta,
  Robin
    P.S.  Anyone have Bill Gates' phone number?

>A few auxlang ideas seem to work well. My own favorites are

>Boiled down to a few technical points:
>
>* light part-of-speech marking
>* small but powerful set of roots
>* subject-verb-object phrase order
>* consistent affixes/compounds
>* mostly CV, no consonant clusters
>* recognizable mnemonic vocabulary
>* few rules and no exceptions
>

    With Glosa the Part-of-Speech markers are notably absent, unnecessary,
or missing.  Which, I can't be sure; but, up to now, I have been working on
the premise that the proper placement of a word in a sentence was all the
signposting its function needed.
    There is a chance that owing to a quirk of nature, syntax seems natural
to me, and does not to many others.  On the other hand, nurture could have
played a part - as a result of my learning Parsing and Analysis, as a
primary-school boy, in Queensland.
    The question of Glosa's occasional awkward, consonant-rich words,
mostly of Greek origin, can easily be solved through a deft use of the
culling knife.

>I've been able to successfully read/write/speak only Occidental.

    Somehow, the tolerance, shown by the authors, of variation between, and

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Tue, 25 Jul 2000 09:12:30 -0700


> Yes, but I'm trying to grasp how effective Lingua Franca was,
> whether a bare facilitator of cross-cultural communication, or a
> flexible language which could facilate, for example, precise
> explication of a theory.

Flexible it was, but precise it was not. All the verbs were
marked by -r, which sounds ridiculous now to Romance speakers
but sure made it easy to parse. Plurals in -s, sometimes a
past participle in -t, most other words ended in random vowels.

Science generates its own nomenclature, which is somewhat
international but equally barbaric, e.g. species names
and astronomical designations. An IAL should be at the
service of the common people, plain and common-sensical.
Nothing on Earth is as practical as trade and conquest ...

> I'm slightly doubtful that LF spawned Tok Pisin.

Sailors who spoke it carried it everywhere, like plague.
Development of a creole merely required enslaving diverse
populations and setting brutal ignorant bosses over them,
effectively stifling their use of native languages.
LF provided some vocabulary, universal creolization
processes did the rest. AAUI.

> If my understanding is correct, LF has been lost and its detailed
> structure is a matter of speculation.  It had a simpler grammar
> than French (though it called itself the French language).  But
> was it as grammatically simple as the ideal creole or pidgin?

Bickerton described an "ideal" (nobody agrees 100% on this).
It "should" be SVO and rely on particles for tense/aspect/mood,
virtually no flexions or affixes, and minimal vocabulary.

LF was far from ideal, but was as simple as language can be.
It had no fixed structure, but had one criterion (only):
If and when a statement is understood, it is correct.
The ideal language for pirates, slaves, and tourists!

I like the idea of a pure-ideal pidgin/creole as IAL.
To be even easier for European speakers, it should
use Latin roots. Glosa is not so far from that.

(Perhaps as corporate enslavement of the world proceeds,
the conditions for creole formation will return soon?
I listen to 20-year olds speaking, and shudder.)

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Solution to Glosa's Vocab problem
From: Paul O Bartlett <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>
Date: Tue, 25 Jul 2000 20:49:05 -0400 (EDT)

On Tue, 25 Jul 2000, Michael Talbot-Wilson wrote (excerpted):

> At 1999-07-25 09:20 -0400, Jay Bowks wrote:

> > The original Interglossa idea was to use a minimum
> > set of roots as in Basic English but derive these from

    Basic English, of course, was/is basically a fraud.  Yes, it had a
list of 850 words, but it used word-groups such as English's highly
notorious phrasal verbs which cannot entirely be decomposed lexically
into their constituents.  Therefore, the actual working set of
functional lexical units which had to be memorized was far greater than
850.

> > I do agree that 1000 words is just bare minimum. And
> > 1200 to 2500 words would be more usable; [...]
>
> But on the Basic principle, shouldn't it be possible, with that
> bare minimum or whatever is on the Net (Charles says it is 1800 -
> I didn't count them), to hold these discussions in the
> language?  Why are we talking English?

    For the record, I compiled the "Glosa Basic Reference" which was,
and may still be, the primary word list on the world wide web.  I sent
the preliminary version in hard copy to G.E.O. for their consent to put
up.  I had noticed that the printed lists of "Glosa 1000" were not
consistent in their content.  So, using some rudimentary computer
utilities, I collated the lists and added a handful of words (probably
not more than twenty or so) from other Glosa sources.  Then I formatted
it into an HTML document.  Because the only browser I had available at
the time was a little primitive and did not do tables, I adopted the
text-only format that I did.  Because of the way the lists (G-E/E-G)
were structured, I never did count the words exactly, but they were
about 1600.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Paul O Bartlett <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>
Date: Tue, 25 Jul 2000 20:58:43 -0400 (EDT)

On Tue, 25 Jul 2000, Charles wrote (excerpt):

> I like the idea of a pure-ideal pidgin/creole as IAL.
> To be even easier for European speakers, it should
> use Latin roots. Glosa is not so far from that.

    Pham Xuan Thai's Lingua Sistemfrater (or just Frater) was rather
Glosa-like but used more Latin roots than Glosa, as best I recall
without digging out my copy.  (And it used only thirteen consonants and
five vowels.)  Actually, I think Hogben was too optimistic in his
assessment of understanding of Greek roots.  A lot of people --
including many of those worldwide who use the "International Scientific
Vocabulary" -- may *use* words with Greek roots more or less as lexical
wholes without thereby having a very good knowledge of what the atomic
roots themselves mean.  Those of us with a modicum of English etymology
and a smattering of Latin and classical Greek may know some of them,
but I suspect that even your average scientist may not know them well.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Michael Talbot-Wilson <mtw{at}birdseye{dot}view{dot}net{dot}au>
Date: Wed, 26 Jul 2000 12:02:51 +0930 (CST)


> I like the idea of a pure-ideal pidgin/creole as IAL.

Using separate words in place of the number inflexion of the noun
or the tense inflexions of the verb seems perfectly obvious and
easy.  But I'm not certain that there is any advantage in it.

That Glosa claims it but doesn't fully do it is a bit strange.
And after Peano, Interlingua grew grammar, also a bit strange.

And regular inflexions are also perfectly obvious and easy.  Isn't
this fact implied by the other one?  Is there any essential
difference?  Isn't is purely a matter of orthography?  If it is
agreed that the "ge-" prefix is an inflexion (is it?), is anything
achieved by making "ge" an autonomous word, and pretending we have
dispensed with inflexions?

Entire avoidance of morphological change in derivation is more
problematic for me.  Do you not lose clarity or precision at this
point, unless supported by numerous traditional phrases?

If I say "Quick!", you need context to begin to know whether I
mean "Come quick!" or "Wasn't that quick!".  If I say "Quickly!",
perhaps it is just idiom that excludes "Didn't he come quickly!"
Or maybe it is an obscure rule of phraseology.  Do we need a
catalog of such rules, to mirror them in the pure ideal
pidgin/creole?

Maybe a change of word form in derivation is just helper wheels.
But when you can't ride, aren't helper wheels nice!"

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Analysis and synthesis
From: Michael Talbot-Wilson <mtw{at}birdseye{dot}view{dot}net{dot}au>
Date: Wed, 26 Jul 2000 13:05:02 +0930 (CST)

Thinking of patterns of derivation, used in one IAL and
not in another, suggests the idea of redundancy, things that are
avoidable but may be helpful.  Or may be a hindrance.  Helper
wheels.

They may be a help because the beginner needs or wants extra to
make out the meaning and to create the meaning.  And the market of
an IAL is always beginners, rather than native speakers.

They may be a hindrance because there are more things that you
"have" to get right.  Redundancy means that it doesn't matter if
you get a few things wrong.  Still, if there is more stuff you
feel you "have" to get right, that is a problem.

The Glosa tense marking seems to have an advantage over inflexions
which have no redundancy.  It's not as easy to be wrong with "pa",
you put it there or you don't.  It's easier to be wrong if the
present ending is "-as" and the past ending is "-is", a single
vowel change, easy to overlook.

In practice, is this a real advantage, or a real problem?  I guess
it doesn't make much difference.

If inflexions were banished from Engish we might have something
like:  The ing move finger do write and after did write do move
on.  In some languages the adjective may optionally precede or
follow the noun.  The tense marker, adverb or whatver it is could
do the same, so we could have "moveing" as well as "ing move".

To put it another way:  The anta move finger as write and after
is write as move on.  If there is a present marker as well as a
past marker there is no advantage over inflexion.  It is necessary
to say:  The ing move finger write and after did write move on.

Hmm... bottle's empty already.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] new word list, RFC
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Wed, 26 Jul 2000 10:40:04 -0700


I would like to compile a new word list, as described
in previous messages the past week or two, with the
help and advice of any interested persons. It would be
placed on a web page, in the "public domain", etc etc.

It would need to grow slowly and be regularly updated
and maintained. At first it might have just the basic
Glosa and Ido roots, but I'd like to add Latin,
Occidental, and any others that people want.
This would require a fair bit of cooperative work.

Perhaps the Glosa 6000 could be added with password access;
I wouldn't want to offend the surviving Glosa author or
violate copyrights. Maybe the project should be passworded
anyway during the early stages of development.

I think the BNC English list would serve best for the
"key" field, but in database terms there cannot be any
truly acceptable "unique key" because of polysemy etc.
Still, having the English and Latin and perhaps other
natlang equivalents should make it clear enough to humans.
This approach worked well in the Novial Lexike and the
OCC Radicarium, neither of which is (fully) online.

To reduce the scope of the project to practicality,
it should focus on the most essential 5000 or so
roots likely to be present in an effective IAL.
The best lists AFAIK were listed earlier.

I am not decided exactly how it should be done, and
would appreciate hearing comments from anyone interested,
or any offers of assistance. Please send any such
replies directly to me rather than to the email list.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge- (Writing)
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Thu, 27 Jul 2000 08:03:09 +1000

At 20:25 22/07/2000 +0930, Michael Talbot-Wilson wrote:

>>   .....
>> In which case, what's your objection to the ge- again?
>
>Vide plu su lexi per mi tange facili de komence ski.  Co pa es
>demonstra es ne nece es in plu glosa per natura.  Plu skema tange
>plu IAL pa inklude ne sine ma face uniformi, sed ko-co face
>elementa per bali ab pan qod es ne nece.
>
*   Well, Friends,
    After spending a serious amount of time trying to disambiguate
Michael's Glosa I gave up.
    The message seems to be saying that if one reads what Michael has said,
above, they will see where he lightly touches on a beginning of knowing.
    All that is needed - for the expression of the participle (I assume) -
is already in Glosa, by its very nature.
    The plans, included, to touch the IAL, will not do so without being
made more uniform, but, having said all that, (we should) simplify by
throwing out all that is not needed.

    Michael, had you included an English-language rendition (in square
brackets) after your Glosa, we could have treated the situation as a
training exercise, as well as one of information; I would have suggested
alternative - non-English-idiomatic - ways of saying what you intended to
communicate.


     So, here is a personal note from me: there is a serious difficulty
with Glosa:-
much time can be spent in editing, and attempting to correct, Glosa that
trans-scribe national language idiom.

     Glosa IS fairly easy to write, BUT, at the start, I ask learners to
edit heavily: read OUT ALOUD what you have written, so that you hear the
Glosa - not the 'national-language' translation that comes from your inner
voice.  Ask yourself, "Do I understand, directly, the Glosa words that I am
hearing, without the need to consciously translate them into my national
language?"
     When the answer is, 'No,' then you are probably hearing your
national-language idioms transferred into Glosa, and the resulting Glosa
that you have written is not following the rules of logical syntax: recast
the sentence using simple, direct language, of the "dog bites man" variety,
and re-read the edited version to HEAR the Glosa again.  When you
understand, directly, the Glosa you are hearing, then you are writing good
Glosa.
     I would suggest attempting complex phrases and clauses after you are
confident that your simple Glosa sentences are being understood.

Saluta,
  Robin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Re: Scooping, Always Scooping
From: Joseph Carson <samizdata{at}earthlink{dot}net>
Date: Wed, 26 Jul 2000 16:23:44 -0700

Robin wrote: "... if Novial/Ido/Interlingua/Occidental crowd had been able to
reach consensus mode, they would have ^scooped the pot^ long ago."

Here's a classic case in point of how illustrative idioms can be less than
transparent among contemporary speakers of the same language, even when their
basic meanings can be guessed at from the context of their usage to make a
point. What is the pithy expression "scoop the pot" itself derived from?  As a
Pacrim Yank, many of the coinages that I've come across on this fascinating
list are foreign to me, but not so impenetrable as to be entirely
incomprehensible.  I do have dialectological interests also, though, that
extend of necessity beyond the purport of any analogy as such to its origin in
the culture of the area at large, "pot scooping" being a perfect example of
the phenomenon. And so what, pray tell, _is_ the basis for this beguiling
expression? Give the a bone, lads ... I need to learn a bit more 'Strine than
"no worries, throw another shrimp on the barbie, mate!" (which I understand is
never actually used by Ozyz) after all; God knows you have more than enough of
a reason to mock us Californians as fault-zone barbarians as it is, but use
this as a chance to prove that we are not all uniformly ineducable on a bet,
okay?  See if I can "parse my arse" past this one (or _not_!), dudes ...
Surf's up! - Rgrds, Joe

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Wed, 26 Jul 2000 16:48:55 -0700


>     With Glosa the Part-of-Speech markers are notably absent, unnecessary,
> or missing.  Which, I can't be sure; but, up to now, I have been working on
> the premise that the proper placement of a word in a sentence was all the
> signposting its function needed.

Adding u/plu and pa/nu etc particles helps a lot, though
it can get ugly if overdone. Prepositions are still difficult,
but all the auxlangs have too many prepositions for my taste.

>     The question of Glosa's occasional awkward, consonant-rich words,
> mostly of Greek origin, can easily be solved through a deft use of the
> culling knife.

There are fewer of those in Glosa than most other auxlangs.
(Is the butcher on-duty yet?)

> >I've been able to successfully read/write/speak only Occidental.

>     I would like to know which aspect of the language suits your particular
> language skills and propensities.  Have you identified what it is about
> Occidental that made it ^click^ with you?

Mainly it's 2 things, both catering to laziness:

1. The analytic verb phrase structure (auxiliary verb ha/es/fa
preceding main verb) fairly similar to Glosa's pattern.

2. The recognizability of the vocabulary (basically, the latinate
portion of English) which Glosa does not match up to.

> Attempts to rationalise the lexicon, through
> action on the Internet, only revealed further undiscovered linguistic
> confusion;

Could be fixed ...

> >I think Glosa is dying, despite so much interest and potential;

[more melodrama snipped]

> *   This is a highly-charged final paragraph.  I trust, as in the movies,
> such a question-filled ending forshadows a sequel!!!
>     Watch this space.

I hope the hero's name isn't Godot.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Paul O Bartlett <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>
Date: Wed, 26 Jul 2000 20:02:50 -0400 (EDT)

On Tue, 25 Jul 2000, Robin Gaskell wrote (excerpted):

>     I do apologise to all, but with civilisation under threat, I have had
> to 'de-prioritise' the Glosa work.

>                                     The 'promise' has not been taken up -
> in a world hell-bent on conspicuous consumption and a self-destructive,
> aggressive competition for what is left of the Planet's resources.  There
> seems no time for people to contemplate ideas that are simply worthwhile,
> but not immediately profitable.

    Robin, I for one think that you are dead wrong in your gloomy,
sour, anti-capitalist diatribes.  Can we stick to Glosa here?

>     While I cannot speak for Bodmer and Hogben, nor for their 'scathing'
> attitude towards other auxlangs, I can say, from my discussions with Ron
> Clark, that he never published any critical comment about other designed
> languages.

    This is good.  It is one things to discuss various aspect of a
conIAL.  It is another, and an unproductive other, to go around trying
to tear things down.  The Auxlang list has heard quite enough of this
sort of dialog of the deaf.

>                                                 While designing the
> 'speak-as-you-look-it-up-in-the-dictionary' language,

    Which I think is unrealistic, simply because people with different
native habits are going to look things up in different orders.  To one
person, SVO seems "natural" and "the way people think."  But the next
person says, "No, SOV is truly natural and truly the way people think."
And so on.

>                                                       Ron Clark also had
> the good sense to ensure that both the appearance and the sound of the
> phrasing was pleasant, as well as making sense.

    A plus.

>       Regarding the rules, and there is basically only one - a word is
> modified by its precedent - Ron was horrified at the thought that learners
> should sit down and internalise a grammar book of rules; children certainly
> don't.  So Ron decreed that people would speak Glosa without first studying
> a Grammar book.  In fact, he virtually banned the idea of producing such a
> guide.

    In this I think he was only partially right.  While it is true that
children do not ordinarily learn from a formal grammar text, neither do
they learn free from context, including nonverbal cues.  The
paradigmatic sentence approach is all well and good as far as it goes,
but the problem is that it does not go far enough.  The context is
missing.  Connected text more than just isolated sentences is needed in
order to show, for example, that incessant marking for things like
plurality, tense, and aspect are simply not necessary in all cases, no
matter how much monoglot anglophones have been bamboozled by English
into thinking so.  And so on.

> 'speaking-as-they-looked-it-up-in-the-dictionary'.

    Again, this is based on an invalid assumption which, in my opinion,
can only lead to problems.

>      The evaluation of the situation requires more intelligent thinking:
> which language, if adopted to serve as the world's IAL, will most benefit
> the human race, this is the correct question.

    However, there is the position that there need not be one, single
international auxiliary language, one-size-fits-all.

Subject: [glosalist] SIL Lex Consid for Tok Pisin (Re: [glosalist] Ge- (Writing)
From: "Jay Bowks" <jjbowks{at}adam{dot}cheshire{dot}net>
Date: Tue, 27 Jul 1999 12:05:34 -0400

From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
>http://org/sil/www/silewp/1998/002/SILEWP1998-002.html#section_6
>
Que? That address won't get you anywhere...
Here's the URL: http://www.sil.org/silewp/1998/002/
Summer Institute of Linguistics Electronic Working Papers
(SILEWP 1998-002)

     Author:
Karl J. Franklin

     Title:
Lexicography Considerations for Tok Pisin

     Abstract:
          This article examines a number of issues that are involved in
dictionary
          compilation, including terminology, elicitation, kinds of
meanings, lexical
          relationships, componential analysis and definitions. Definitions
may be both
          folk and technical and the methodology suggested here profits from
the
          semantic framework and theory of Wierzbicka (1985, 1986, 1987), as
well
          as from other sources. Illustrations are given in Tok Pisin as
well as in
          English. The article concludes with a number of practical
suggestions which
          are relevant in compiling dictionaries in the vernacular languages
of Papua
          New Guinea.

Date created:
25-Mar-1998

Copyright © 1998 Summer Institute of Linguistics, Inc.

`See my above words touching ease of learning.  The thing we are
discussing, the "just-said", (the prefix sign of the past
participle) has been shown to be unnecessary in natural languages.
Design of (touching) artificial languages has included not only
making them regular but also making them simple by discarding
everything that is unnecessary.'

The word list didn't contain "learn", thus "easy begin to know"
for ease of learning.  Likewise "only", which I synthesized as
"without more" (sine ma).  Also, "my" was missing, thus "by
means of me" (per mi).

>     The message seems to be saying that if one reads what Michael has said,
> above, they will see where he lightly touches on a beginning of knowing.

I meant "dealing with", "about", "concerning".  The word list
contains a set of "associatives", one of which is "touching" =
"tange".  It was the nearest I could find to "about".

>     All that is needed - for the expression of the participle (I assume) -
> is already in Glosa, by its very nature.

> made more uniform, but, having said all that, (we should) simplify by
> throwing out all that is not needed.

Seems to show that it is hard to get by without grammar, without
some way of showing how the words stand in relation to each
other.  That a succession of words as you find them in the
dictionary doesn't work.

Admittedly, that is a spectacular rush to judgement.  It was my
first ignorant attempt to write in Glosa.

>     Michael, had you included an English-language rendition (in square
> brackets) after your Glosa, we could have treated the situation as a
> training exercise, as well as one of information; I would have suggested

> communicate.

Yes, I guessed that there had to be something like that.  How many
such phrases are there in Glosa?  Would a completely-defined
grammar such as that of Esperanto or Ido avoid the need for them?
You just wrote something about
"speaking-as-you-look-it-up-in-the-dictionary", which is what I
tried to do.  Can it work?  Without also looking up patterns in a
phrase-pattern book?

>      So, here is a personal note from me: there is a serious difficulty
> with Glosa:-
> much time can be spent in editing, and attempting to correct, Glosa that
> trans-scribe national language idiom.
>
>      Glosa IS fairly easy to write, BUT, at the start, I ask learners to
> edit heavily: read OUT ALOUD what you have written, so that you hear the
> Glosa - not the 'national-language' translation that comes from your inner
> voice.  Ask yourself, "Do I understand, directly, the Glosa words that I am
> hearing, without the need to consciously translate them into my national
> language?"
>      When the answer is, 'No,' then you are probably hearing your
> national-language idioms transferred into Glosa, and the resulting Glosa
> that you have written is not following the rules of logical syntax: recast
> the sentence using simple, direct language, of the "dog bites man" variety,
> and re-read the edited version to HEAR the Glosa again.  When you
> understand, directly, the Glosa you are hearing, then you are writing good
> Glosa.
>      I would suggest attempting complex phrases and clauses after you are
> confident that your simple Glosa sentences are being understood.

Okay.  Will need to think about that.  Again, it's not quite what
I understood you to be saying in another message.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge- (Writing)
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Wed, 26 Jul 2000 21:12:37 -0700


> Design of (touching) artificial languages has included not only
> making them regular but also making them simple by discarding
> everything that is unnecessary.'

Less is more. That's a good design principle.

> The word list didn't contain "learn", thus "easy begin to know"

A full list of common English words translated,
that would make it easy for the all-important beginner.

Of course many terms would be compounds; a minimal
vocabulary cannot afford separate roots for each of:
learn, teach, instruct, study, review, know, understand,
school, professor, etc. Glosa is like Tok Pisin, and there
is a web page showing how to build words without affixes:

http://org/sil/www/silewp/1998/002/SILEWP1998-002.html#section_6

> I meant "dealing with", "about", "concerning".  The word list
> contains a set of "associatives", one of which is "touching" =
> "tange".  It was the nearest I could find to "about".

Prepositions are trickiest part of Ido too, I think.
Some languages prefer to use verbs, like "concerning".
But that doesn't seem to be the Greek/Glosa way: "peri".

> Seems to show that it is hard to get by without grammar, without
> some way of showing how the words stand in relation to each
> other.  That a succession of words as you find them in the
> dictionary doesn't work.

Hogben referred to the Chinese concept of "full" and "empty" words.
Normal dictionary words, nouns/verbs/adjectives, are "full".
Particles are "empty" but they implement much of the the grammar.
How else to mark a phrase boundary? (taking preps as particles)

> Admittedly, that is a spectacular rush to judgement.  It was my
> first ignorant attempt to write in Glosa.

That's nothing, I can write worse Glosa without even trying.

> You just wrote something about
> "speaking-as-you-look-it-up-in-the-dictionary", which is what I
> tried to do.  Can it work?  Without also looking up patterns in a
> phrase-pattern book?

I would call that "relexing" and it is a major strength of Glosa
that direct relexing can work as far as it does. With a good
"phrase list" and just a few examples you would have succeeded.

Let's see: "ko (u) boni lexi-seria lista e xxx? (plu) oligo exempla"
I made up "word-series" for "phrase", and couldn't find "just/only".
Not so bad overall, eh?

It should be possible to automate this word-lookup process.
Type in English, output decent-enough Glosa. I used to have
something like that, going from Glosa to English. Hmm.

http://catty.com/~catty/lang/dict.cgi

*   Unable to comment properly on Michael's message, I would say what I can about the ~ge-~ form:-

   . yes, it is an abbreviation of ~gene~,

   . Passive Voice was not considered a necessity by Ron Clark, originally, and was excluded from Glosa,

   . it was used to create the participle form, much as "-ed" or "-en" in English,

   . ~gene~ does seem the natural VERB to the Passive Voice, even though to say ~gene ge-X~ [=to be Xed] does seem like a mouthful,

   . I am in favour of Glosa having a specific Passive form, and know that this hyphenated affix does look like an inflection, though it is more of a "flag",

   . in different situations other VERBS than ~gene~ can be used,

   . while Ron Clark did derive the affix from ~gene~, he did say that it represented an interesting coincidence, with German also using #ge-# to form its Passive form.


Saluta,

  Robin

>an amateur.  You might do well to take your facts from something written

>within the decade, at least, and by an expert.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge- (Writing)
From: Michael Talbot-Wilson <mtw{at}birdseye{dot}view{dot}net{dot}au>
Date: Thu, 27 Jul 2000 17:31:33 +0930 (CST)


> That's nothing, I can write worse Glosa without even trying.
>
> > You just wrote something about
> > "speaking-as-you-look-it-up-in-the-dictionary", which is what I
> > tried to do.  Can it work?  Without also looking up patterns in a
> > phrase-pattern book?
>
> I would call that "relexing" and it is a major strength of Glosa
> that direct relexing can work as far as it does. With a good
> "phrase list" and just a few examples you would have succeeded.

But wouldn't I succeed as quickly, perhaps more quickly, with the
five-page "complete grammar reference" (really three pages plus
lists of affixes) of another IAL, one that does not pretend to
have no grammar, but instead, completely defines and precisely
states the grammar?

The problem I had was with the lack of definition, the fact that I
had to guess how do signify "to learn" rather than "learning", and
"does not have to be" rather than "must not".  If the words are
invariable there needs to be a book of idioms, phrase patterns.
How big a book?  If it is less than five pages, where is it?

And five pages of phrases, one per line, would be huge compared
with five pages of grammar tables.  Really, it must be a single
page.  It should be easy to put that page in the Net.  Yet it is
not there, it seems.

So, what is the vital gleam that makes Glosa the way forward?

I understand from what you (Charles) have said that only
sufficient grammar to disambiguate the communication is disclosed.
That is the advantage over a tense inflexion.  You can say "pa"
but can skip it sometimes, and almost never need to specifically
state that the verb is in the present tense.

I can understand that that leads to snappy and efficient
communication.  A language that always has to cart endings around
may become a little tedious.

That might be an important advantage of the perfect first
language, but when we are talking about an IAL, an eternal second
language, to be forever newly-encountered by adults, used in
international communication, writing international treaties and so
on, perhaps that advantage of brevity should be traded for a clear
definition of a regular grammar.

So I hope that is not it, the key factor.  Incidentally, it seems
to be shared with Peano's Interlingua.

Something that I have noticed is that some IALs differentiate
themselves by taking some factor, good in itself, to an extreme.
E.g. Occidental seems to sacrifice all else for the dream of
at-sight readability by Europeans only.  Is the grammarlessness of
Glosa, or the creoleness of Glosa a similar enthusiasm, good in
its way, but ultimately too monochromatic?

plu tu        Tvoi           your
u vi            Vash        your
u tu            Tvoy        your
matematika matematika mathematics

Alex.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge- (Writing)
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Thu, 27 Jul 2000 16:03:36 -0700


> > I would call that "relexing" and it is a major strength of Glosa

> But wouldn't I succeed as quickly, perhaps more quickly, with the
> five-page "complete grammar reference" (really three pages plus
> lists of affixes) of another IAL,

I doubt it, because I have trouble keeping my eyes open
scanning the list of Ido affixes, for example. It is easy
to grasp the system of it, but very difficult to use.
And remember Ido was simplified from Esperanto, relying
much less on extended agglutination. Still it's too much.
Gode's interlingua abandons that systematic affixation.

Glosa has less grammar than the others, so its system
should be more easily grasped if presented properly.
But what is "the" proper way? I think many ways should
be available, because one size cannot fit all people.
Let's have more varied Glosa grammar descriptions
on the web, and see what works best for whom.

> The problem I had was with the lack of definition, the fact that I
> had to guess how do signify "to learn" rather than "learning",

You're right, the presentation needs to be improved.
I think it would be a good group project, anyone could
make a web page or two, and discuss them further here.
Let's start a movement! Revolt!!! Er ... sorry.
Syd's is very good.

> And five pages of phrases, one per line, would be huge compared
> with five pages of grammar tables.  Really, it must be a single
> page.  It should be easy to put that page in the Net.  Yet it is
> not there, it seems.

Maybe it's better to have more easy-to-read Glosa examples,
maybe in the form of short stories; not polished, just simple.
I found that reading the many issues of Cosmoglotta, a magazine
written in Occidental, was most effective in teaching the eyes
how to parse the language without thinking of grammar at all.

Recent neurological studies show that a large percentage of
language processing is very low-level visual pattern matching,
developed only by extensive practice. I had the experience of
a sudden easing of effort and yet with greater understanding,
after about a week or two of mere reading.

You may benefit from the formal order of systematic grammar,
but most people can't do that. They need a different method.
Why economize on web pages? Let's have hundreds more,
all different, there's no center to the fabric of the web.

> I understand from what you (Charles) have said that only
> sufficient grammar to disambiguate the communication is disclosed.

Sure, maximize easiness for speaker/writer and listener/reader.

> That might be an important advantage of the perfect first
> language, but when we are talking about an IAL, an eternal second
> language, to be forever newly-encountered by adults, used in
> international communication, writing international treaties and so
> on, perhaps that advantage of brevity should be traded for a clear
> definition of a regular grammar.

We can have both. Lawyers can always remember to add all
the unnecessary particles, and repeat it 16 more times,
and then translate it anyway into 25 languages.
Actually, I think a language for *common* people is needed;
the upper classes do not need an auxlang, may not want one.
A clear grammatical description would be wonderful, though.
Much is now written about pidgin/creoles, let's tap into that.

> So I hope that is not it, the key factor.

The key factor is that people really have fun with this one.
It is liberating to dispense with (most of) annoying grammar.
And yet it does work as a full language, with little effort.
(But I don't recant that serious updates to Glosa are needed.)

> E.g. Occidental seems to sacrifice all else for the dream of
> at-sight readability by Europeans only.

It does cater to eurolang speakers. Worked great on me.
OCC is extensible though, not ossified/frozen. I like
regional IALs, one for Europe, another for east Asia.
Swahili and Malay are kinda like that. IALs are no dream,
they are common throughout all of history.

Perhaps the problem is too many would-be language-tinkerers,
such as one finds on AUXLANG, who think their grammar tweaks
will save a grateful world or something. There is no need to
invent water when we are swimming in it. (But is it fresh?)
Let's be lazy, take what exists, simplify it a bit, be happy.

> Is the grammarlessness of
> Glosa, or the creoleness of Glosa a similar enthusiasm, good in
> its way, but ultimately too monochromatic?

Glosa may be more internationally easy-to-learn than others.
No big advantage to Europeans, easier pronunciation for most.
The ideal global language *should* be colorless, unbiased;
let people provide all the color.
