To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge- (Writing)
From: Paul O Bartlett <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>
Date: Thu, 27 Jul 2000 20:56:23 -0400 (EDT)

On Thu, 27 Jul 2000, Charles wrote (excerpted):

> Glosa has less grammar than the others,

    I do hope that people are not confusing grammar with inflectional
or agglutinative morphology as if they were synonymous.  A language
which has no grammar is not a language.

> You're right, the presentation needs to be improved.

    I think that Glosa's greatest weakness, so to speak, has been its
presentation.  At the risk of blowing my own horn, I would refer people
to my essay, "Thoughts on IAL Success" at
http://www.smart.net/~bartlett/thoughts.html

> You may benefit from the formal order of systematic grammar,
> but most people can't do that. They need a different method.

    I think there should be both, paradigmatic examples for those to
whom that is the better approach and some sort of more formalized
grammar exposition for those who prefer and can make use of it.

> Actually, I think a language for *common* people is needed;
> the upper classes do not need an auxlang, may not want one.

    Perhaps more than one IAL on more than one level.  I did once
exchange two letters in Glosa with a man in China.  They were not
particularly deep, but casual communication is often like that.

> (But I don't recant that serious updates to Glosa are needed.)

    My opinion has been for quite some time that the main things Glosa
needs are better wordlists and better promotion.  I don't really have
a lot of problem with the structure itself.

> Perhaps the problem is too many would-be language-tinkerers,
> such as one finds on AUXLANG, who think their grammar tweaks
> will save a grateful world or something.

    In the end, it is probably *not* linguistic characteristics which
make or break a conIAL.  After all, look at Esperanto. ;-)  We can
tinker and diddle to our heart's content to strive ever after the will
o' the wisp of the "perfect" IAL, and at the end of the day we may not
necessarily be much farther down the road.  Once an IAL is good enough,
other factors come into play.  Sooner or later the tinkering just has
to stop.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] dictionary, was Re: fingertalk (for deaf)*
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Fri, 28 Jul 2000 22:25:37 +1000

At 22:52 23/07/2000 +0200, Marcel Springer wrote:
>Karo Charles,
> > I think Glosa is dying, despite so much interest and potential;
>
>Tu plu grama habe alo tem spe alo tem no-spe pro Glosa.  Place ne
>lose koragi!
>
*   Yes Marcel,
   I will agree, I feel that the Glosa project does have validity and
should be continued!
> > They could be scored by frequency of usage and stratified
> > into "top 1000", "basic 2500", and "full 6000".
> > In basic lists the less-favored synonyms can be dropped.
>
>The internet dictionary project of W. Patterson/ R. Gaskell/ P.
>Bartlett (www.neosplice.com/~ailanto/glosa.htm) goes in that
>direction.  Let us improve this one before starting another
>project.
>
*   This project should be revived, and completed.

> > So, I would like to have a table of Kilgariff roots
> > crossed with auxlangs sorta like so:
> >
> > Kilga   LF     Glosa        Ido   Novial  Occid  Latin
> > basket  gorba  korba,mirso  korb  korb    corb   corbis
>
>This may be helpful.  My suggestion for strategy:
>
>You certainly know Interlingua de Peano/ Latino sine flexione
>(see www.geocities.com/Athens/Olympus/2948/).  It is a direct
>forerunner of Glosa and nearly the same as Glosa.  Main difference:
>Glosa has a limited (= good) but unsatisfying vocabulary.  Interlingua
>has no own vocabulary.  It uses the Latin one and a simple word
>derivation scheme instead.  With this scheme, - a bit refined -, we
>could improve Glosa words.
>
>Examples:
>  Glosa words: "silva" and "silvi" for forest, wood
>   .....
*   This is the bit where I have trouble.
    Considering that present Glosa users, dotted around the world , are
already kitted out with Glosa 1000 lists and _Glosa 6000_ dictionaries,
unless we can come up with a way of sending them REPLACEMENT dictionaries,
then, further fundamental research into lexicon formation will prove to be
confusing, if not divisive.

    Can we look at a balance sheet of the benefit of further fundamental
changes in the spelling of the Glosa lexicon, versus the the cost in
frustration and loos of the user-base?
    This does not mean that we should not go ahead with the
standardisation, addition of missing words, and general trimming of surplus
synonyms .. leading to the production of a _Glosa 2500_ Learner's Dictionary.
    Such rationalisation can be incorporated fairly readily into the
G-E.ALL and E-G.ALL files; however, for people without access to the Net or
to a computer, it seems only right that such Glosa supporters should be
provided with the Upgrade (2500) dictionary.  Somehow, the  Glosa 2500
dictionary, with both G->E and E->G sections, must be printed and made
available to all Glosa-pe.  This cost will probably be bourne by me;
however, the formation of finalised lists can be done by all on Glosalist.

    Before there are radical changes, however, Wendy Ashby must be involved
in the plans: this is a matter for diplomacy; while Wendy is the the
remaining Author of Glosa, we have to consider the future of the language.
I cannot become involved in a power struggle for "control" of Glosa: it is
my hope that future changes to the language will be achieved through a
consensus of all involved.

>   Latin:       silva, ae, f., ablativus silva, then ...:
>  Peano:       silva
>  --->         "silva" is good, "silvi" is bad and should be
>                 eliminated.
>(I started to make a list of those bad synonyms, will post them later
>for discussion.)
>
*   The word "bad" here, means not aligned with other derivatives from the
original Latin root.  Now, this "i" could have been a mistake, or the
result of much consideration by Ron and Wendy; I feel that thhe thinking by
Ron and Wendy needs to be taken into account before specific changes to the
spelling of words is decided.
    A reason for not wanting to see the promotion of numerous spelling
changes to Glosa is the disturbance it will cause to the existing
user-base.  I recall, all too clearly, the purtubing effect on me, during
the 80's, of regular, fundametal changes to the Glosa lexicon.  My
intuitive feeling is that numerous changes to the lexicon, in the present,
will, on balance, be counter-productive.

> > Glosa roots (= words, usually) tend to a CVCV pattern
> > with the final vowel being meaningless 99% of the time.
>
>I agree.  This is very important!  But do not kill all of them.
>"silv" would be harder to speak (consonant cluster with the following
>word), harder to understand by listening and sounds uglier.
>
*   For this example, the ~lv~ in the middle of the word counts as the
'second consonantal sound'.  Hence  s-i-lv-i  is fundamentally CVCV.

Saluta,
  Robin

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Fri, 28 Jul 2000 23:34:52 +1000

At 10:47 24/07/2000 +0930, Michael Talbot Wilson wrote:
>Patrick Dunn pa skribe:
>   .....
>>
>> Here, let's be mo0re constructive: what if you had absolute control over
>> Glosa?  How would you mark the participle, if not with ge-?
>
>Patrick, I started out with the reasonable question, whether the
>ge- in Glosa could be avoided.  I'm still waiting for an
>answer.   .....
#   Arr, well, yes.  An answer.
    The situation as I see it is that the idea of a "participle" - of
adjectivising the VERB - is a special case, similar to the "gerund" where
the VERB is nouned.

    In Glosa, the gerund was already covered - using the unchanged
substantive word and adding the 'noun flag', ~u~.  The partciple, demanded
by the Passive Voice, however, needed some other trick; was there an
'adjectival flag', or might one have to be invented?  EG

skope   =   look at; to look at; watch
u skope ju-an   =  the watching boy
un andra, skope u ju-an   =  the man, watching the boy
u skope pa es an favori tem-uti   =   watching was his favourite pass-time
an pa hedo skope plu ju-fe, to u mari-margina   =   he enjoyed watching
       (the) girls, at the sea-side
u policia-pe pa vide u ge-skope an, akti u krimi   =   the policeman saw
       the watched man commit the/a crime
u-la andra pa eqa u persona; qi pa gene ge-skope ex u policia-pe  =
        that man was the person who was watched by the policeman

Saluta,
  Robin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Sat, 29 Jul 2000 00:40:21 +1000

At 19:29 23/07/2000 -0700, Charles wrote:
>Patrick Dunn wrote:
>> In any event, I suspect that ge- cannot be circumvented;   .....
>
>It's a very interesting (troublesome) problem.
>   .....
>It looks like the only options would be "ge-" and "qi".
>And there's no present participle BTW IINM.
>   .....
>
>Using a relative clause seems the worst and clumsiest solution,
>and I might even prefer to use participles entirely,
>replacing all relative clauses, with the side-benefit of
>eliminating the *ridiculous* "Semicolon de Hogben".
>
*   Possible:-
       I see the man running away.
  Mi vide un andra; qi kurso ab.  -  I see the man who runs away.
  Mi vide un andra; qi du kurso ab.  -  I see the man, who is running away.
  Mi vide un andra; kurso ab.  - I see the man running away. [rel.pron elided]
  Mi vide un andra, kurso ab.  -  "  "   "   "     "     "  [Charles
preferred]

        She is the running woman.
  Fe eqa u du-kurso femina.  -  She is the running woman.
  Fe eqa u femina; qi kurso.  -  She is the woman who runs.
  Fe eqa u femina; qi du kurso.  -  She is the woman who is running.
  Fe eqa u kurso femina.*      [unsatisfactory]

         He, running, enters the street.
  An, kurso, ki-in u strada.  -  He, running, enters the street.
  An, du-kurso, ki-in u strada.  -  He, running enters the street.
  Du-kurso, an ki-in u strada.  -  Running, he enters the street.
  Kurso, an ki-in u strada.*    [Seems incomplete]
  In u strada, an veni, kurso.  -  Into the street, he comes running.
  In u strada, an veni, du-kurso.  - Into the street he comes running.

>So maybe the thing to do is keep using "ge-", and just
>add a new particle for the present/active participle??
>But they might get mixed up into verb phrases. Oy!!
>
*   Not with suitable punctuation and good syntax.

Saluta,
  Robin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Sat, 29 Jul 2000 00:51:16 +1000

At 22:07 23/07/2000 -0500, Patrick Dunn wrote:

>> Patrick Dunn wrote:
>>
>> > In any event, I suspect that ge- cannot be circumvented;   .....

>And, of course, this is the problem with an absolutely analytic language,
>at least for me.
>
>One could convey present active participles as adjectives, so:
>
>u feli -- the cat
>u skribi feli -- the writing cat
>
>But then, does this perhaps mean "the writing behaves cattishly"?
>
*   Possibly.
    Then, it can be disambiguated by forcing the "continuous tense", thus
using the ~du-~ as the present participle prefix.  EG

  u du-skribi feli  - the writing cat

*   Hi Syd,
    Please do not attempt normal HTML formatting to indent the grammar table.

appears on the Web-page.

<PRE>
 ......
   55555555
   66666666
       @@@@@@@
  ......
</PRE>

   All between <PRE> and </PRE> comes out the same as the file you see on
the page.

OX----------------------------------------------------------------------






                    NOTES TO "CORE"-DICTIONARY
                    ==========================
 OF THE GLOSA-INTERNET-DICTIONARY-PROJECT OF BILL PATTERSON AND OTHERS
 =====================================================================

               Marcel Springer, <m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de>,
                 www.tu-harburg.de/~smms1406/glosa.htm
                          Hamburg, 2000-07-28



0. Index
--------
  1. General Note
  2. Notes to Single Words
  3. Synonyms in Core and Examples of Concepts with More Than One Root
  4. Problems for a German
  5. Core and Glosa 1000



1. General Note
---------------

  You can sort them as you want: bad words remain bad words.  The Glosa
  vocabulary is inconsistent and unsystematical.  All its problems are
  mentioned and discussed in glosalist already.

  Main problems are the
  - arbitrary, random ending vowels (difficult to learn) and
  - having two source languages (Latin and ancient Greek).

  New words should be created.  Marked (e. g. with an asterisk) as not
  original Clark/Ashby/GEO-words, they should replace the 'bad' words.
  Of course, this must be done very carefully.  Wendy Ashby must be
  involved and asked about the consideration work behind these words.



2. Notes to Single Words
------------------------

A list, only catchwords. Alphabetical order.

2.1.  "anti-co" has in Literary-dictionary the meanings "but; however;
        nevertheless; though".  Please give back the Glosa-1000 "sed"
 for "but"!
2.2.  "demi" (half) is French. Latin "semi" (or Greek "hemi") would be
        better.  (Remember "U Lupus"-translation-discussion.)
2.3.  "diptera" (fly) is the animal (the fly), to fly  is "peti" or
        "vola".  "vola" is to be found under "flight". Perhaps write
        "fly {the}" and "fly {to}".
2.4.  "deskende" (descend) not necessary, instead simply: "skende
        kata".
2.5.  "eroti" (physical): error: must be "physical love".
2.6.  "es" (to be) is missing. "onto" instead. Please take Glosa1000's
        "es" again!
2.7.  "kapita": The book "Deutsch-Glosa1000" gives German "Haupt"
        (head, main..).  "Core" gives "cape". That could mean a "high
        point of land going out into the sea" or a "sleeveless garment,
        hanging from the shoulders".  The file "Glosa Basic Reference"
        gives "kapota - bonnet", that would be a synonym to the second.
        "kapota" is not in Literary-dictionary.
2.8.  "ko{n}" wrongly sorted in. Must be treated like "kon" and/or like
        "ko".
2.9.  "kordiali - warm". Not the temperature is meant but an emotion
        (Remember our "U Lupus"-translation-discussion. Cf. Latin "cor,
        cordis" - heart).  It would be better to take "meso-termo -
        warm" and for "kordiali" only "hearty".
2.10. "krati-grega": The book "Deutsch-Glosa1000" gives for "Regierung"
        (government) "krati".  So "krati" is enough, "krati-grega" is
        not necessary.
2.11. "krispa": Core gives "contract". Note: not the "treaty" is meant,
        but to "make shorter; to curl".
2.12. "kritiki-ra":  The book "Deutsch-Glosa 1000" says "kritici" is
        enough for German "Kritik", so "kritiki" would be enough for
 "criticism".
2.13. "mali-dice": "curse" (German "fluchen") is not always "swear"
        (German "schwören").  "I swear I love you." is not "mali".
2.14. Regular comparison is necessary for an auxiliary language. So:
        peiora - worse (schlechter) ==> ma mali
        pesimi - worst (schlechtester, am schlechtesten) ==> maxi mali
        optimi - best (bester, am besten) ==> maxi boni
2.15. "satura" - rich: Latin "satur, satura saturum" is satisfied,
        copious; better for "rich"/wealthy would be "pluto".
2.16. "porta" (harbor) should be "portu", because "porta" is also
        "door" and "to carry".
        (cf. Latin: portus, portus m. - harbur, u-declination, so root
               would be portu;
                    porta, portae f.  - door, a-declination, so root
        would be porta;
             portare           - carry, a-conjugation, so root
        would be porta (too))
2.17. "positi": The book "Deutsch-Glosa 1000" gives German "Posten,
        Stand" (post, place).  For "to put" would be better "loka".
 "loka" is already in Core with "to lay" (see 3.xx).
2.18. "repei" (reply): perhaps typing error, should be "repli".
2.19. "vid-abili": Simpler and better would be "vide-abili".  If we
        take "vid-abili", we should take also "aud-abili" instead of
 "audi-abili" and  "honor-abili" instead "honora-abili".
2.20. For English "first" Glosa has not only "prima" and "proto", but
        also "mo" (in after-position!).  "mo {afterposition}" should be
 supplement to Literary-dictionary, and be prefered for
 Core-dictionary.
2.21. Some words may not so necessary for a beginner's dictionary:
        palpebra (eyelid) (see also 3.60)
 operkula (lid) (see also 3.60)
        runi (rune)
      Other examples could be found, discussed and eliminated.  This
      will be shown by practical use of the dictionary.



3. Synonyms in Core and Examples of Concepts with More Than One Root
--------------------------------------------------------------------

The intention of the internet dictionary project was to get a
beginner's dictionary without synonyms (words with same meaning).

Nevertheless the list below shows synonyms of Core.  This results from
English synonyms and computer sorting.  Some of the synonyms are
"real", (you know the thesis "A language has NO synonyms."), others are
at least so near together, that one of each pair should be removed from
Core and move to a higher level dictionary ("Base" or "Literary").  A
discussion how to treat each word will be necessary.

More tricky is the problem of words with related meanings.  They have
to go back to the same root, but a lot in Core do not.  It could not
be, that we eliminate part-of-speech-markers in Glosa, and then related
words, differing only in part-of-speech, have different roots, only to
distinguish them.  Then an old-fashioned part-of-speech-marker-system
would be better.
(e. g. iloto (dirty) - adjective / tolo (dirt) - noun).

Reading the list below, please keep in mind:
- Sources for Glosa words's meanings are the Richmond lists (and there are
  several different lists, so perhaps you have a list with other meanings)
  and the meanings of the classical origins.
- The author (me) is not a native English speaker.

Now the list:

3.1.  abili [in audi-abili, honora-abili, vid-abili; other words with
       "abili" inside are liabili, probabili, stabili] / pote (able,
       can)
3.2.  acikula (prickly) / aku (nail, needle)
3.3.  ali (any) / uno (some) (see 4.2.)
3.4.  alimenta (feed, food) / cena (dinner, supper) / fago (partake) /
        mangia (eat) / toeno (meal) / viktualia (provisions) /
 ((G100:) vora / sito)
3.5.  anula (circle, circular, ring) / rotundi (round) /
       (Glosa 1000:) rondo
3.6.  aprende (learn) / stude (study)
3.7.  auxi (helpful) / steiligma (support)
3.8.  basili (king) / regi (ruler) / roiali (royal)
3.9.  bulima (hunger) / fami (hungry)
3.10  celera (quick) / rapidi (rapid) / tako (fast)
3.11. cerka (search) / qero (seek)
3.12. certa (certain, sure) / imuni (safety) / sekuri (safe) /
        sura [in: insura (insurance, insure) and in asura (confidence,
 confident)] decide (decide, decision) / resoluti (determined,
 resolute) / resolve (determination)

        selection)

4. Problems for a German
------------------------

4.1 It is not easy to distinguish between:
      hekasto (each, every) / panto (all) / holo (hole) /
        (G1000:) singu.
4.2 The German does not distinguish:
      ali (any) / uno (some)
    ... and an international auxiliary language MUST NOT do either!!!



5. Core and Glosa 1000
----------------------

The discussion in glosalist showed, that here there are two opinions:

The Glosa traditionalists (maybe Robin Gaskell, Nick Hempshall, Marcel
Springer) think, Glosa-1000-words should be prefered. Otherwise the
language would be damaged, the small group of Glosa-users would be
splitted.  A text written with Core-dictionary could not be understood
by a Glosa-1000-learner.

Others (maybe Bill Patterson, Charles) say, Glosa 1000 does not really
exist, because the several 1000-lists contradict each other. They
think, to give Glosa the chance of becoming THE world language, the
best synonym each must be chosen.  These best synonyms are not found
by the taste of two language designers, but by systematical research,
perhaps by comparison of several Romanic languages (Latin, Spain,
Italian, French), other modern languages with many speakers (English,
Russian, German) or other auxiliary language's design drafts (Ido,
Novial, Occidental, Interlingua de IALA) or perhaps by a word
derivation scheme (e. g. Peano's Interlingua/Latina sine Flexione).

Perhaps a compromise between these two groups could be found.

Below there is a list of words of Core-dictionary not in Glosa 1000.

What can we do with it?
- The book "Central Glosa" contains the interesting word list
  "English 5000 to Glosa 1000". It gives more than one meaning to one
  Glosa word (should be 5000/1000=1). With this list some more of the
  Core-words below could be expressed with Glosa 1000 words.
- Some of the words below differ to 1000-words only in the ending
  vowel.  The ending-vowel is a problem on its own (---> start a new
  project).
- Some of the words below may be in one Glosa-1000-list, but not in
  mine.  The contradiction of GEO-word-lists is a problem on its own,
  too.

Now the list: it shows Glosa-word,  Englisch meaning, (German meaning)
(==> Glosa-1000-Wort)]:

5.1.   absenti  absent (abwesend) ==> G1000: ab; ne ci; ne la
5.2.   abstrakti  abstract (abstrakt, Auszug, abstrahieren)
5.3.   acikula  prickly (stachelig)
5.4.   adagio  slow (langsam) ==> G1000: no-tako; lento
5.5.   adeo  goodbye (Leb wohl!)  ==> G1000: vale
5.6.   adhesi  adhere, adhesive (kleben, klebrig)
5.7.   adhesi-ma  paste (Kleister) ==> G1000: pasta
5.8.   adinami  weak (weich) ==> G1000: no-resista; debili
5.9.   adjace  local (örtlich) ==> G1000: lokali
5.10.  adjusta  adjustment (Einstellung, Anordnung)
5.11.  ...
...

  (Stop, stop, stop.  You will kill me, if I would post the whole
  list here.)
...

5.895.  vultu  expression (Ausdruck) ==> G1000: dice klari; emotio
5.896.  zeloso  jealous, jealousy (eifersüchtig, neidisch, Eifersucht,
          Neid)
5.897.  zemia  loss (Verlust)
5.898.  zeo  boil (kochen, sieden)
5.899.  zeugo  team (Team, Gruppe, Arbeitsgruppe, Mannschaft)
          ==> G1000: grega

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] dictionary, was Re: fingertalk (for deaf)*
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Fri, 28 Jul 2000 15:39:48 -0700


> >  --->         "silva" is good, "silvi" is bad and should be
> >                 eliminated.

>     A reason for not wanting to see the promotion of numerous spelling
> changes to Glosa is the disturbance it will cause to the existing
> user-base.

But the final vowels hardly ever matter, and so don't really
need to be spelled "right". They'll all sound like schwa anyway.
Still, they should be etymologically plausible if possible.
There might even be a desirable cross-correlation with the
main vowel, e.g. C-a-CC-e vs. C-o-CC-i (I just made this up),
to make spelling super-easy. Hey, I should patent this "technology"!

> *   For this example, the ~lv~ in the middle of the word counts as the
> 'second consonantal sound'.  Hence  s-i-lv-i  is fundamentally CVCV.

I agree that many of the C-V-CC-V patterns can be pronounced easily
by nearly everyone globally, therefore are acceptable. Actually,
having 3 consonants is better mnemonically and even esthetically.
The final vowel is not significant, so there are under 2000 good
CVCV roots available, but many times more CVCCV's; and it's better
not to use all of them, for easier recognition.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] dictionary
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Fri, 28 Jul 2000 16:28:20 -0700

Marcel Springer wrote:

>     As I wrote, I translated the Core-dictionary into German
>   (URL is www.tu-harburg.de/~smms1406/ggldt.htm).

This is really great work.

>   Main problems are the
>   - arbitrary, random ending vowels (difficult to learn) and
>   - having two source languages (Latin and ancient Greek).

The endings are insignificant and can be ignored in 99% of cases.

Latin is better, but Greek can't be removed, and often
they conflict. I don't know any solution except toleration.

>   New words should be created.  Marked (e. g. with an asterisk) as not
>   original Clark/Ashby/GEO-words, they should replace the 'bad' words.

Add words where necessary, but we must keep all the old too,
as "archaisms". Zamenhoff called it "the method of neologism".
Then let evolution sort it out.

> 2.20. For English "first" Glosa has not only "prima" and "proto", but
>         also "mo" (in after-position!).  "mo {afterposition}" should be
>  supplement to Literary-dictionary, and be prefered for Core-dictionary.

I am unhappy with the whole bunch of u/un/mo/ali/singu/prima/etc words.
Should be "un = one = indefinite", but this will probably never happen.

> 4.2 The German does not distinguish:
>       ali (any) / uno (some)
>     ... and an international auxiliary language MUST NOT do either!!!

Agreed, it is unsavory to me also. Perhaps if the whole set
of words in the determiners/quantifiers group were analyzed,
there could be found enough good words to use, and then leave
the others out of the new "basic" set.

> The Glosa traditionalists (maybe Robin Gaskell, Nick Hempshall, Marcel
> Springer) think, Glosa-1000-words should be prefered. Otherwise the
> language would be damaged,

> Others (maybe Bill Patterson, Charles) say, Glosa 1000 does not really
> exist, because the several 1000-lists contradict each other.

> Perhaps a compromise between these two groups could be found.

Sure, let's just find the 2500 *best* words and call them
the "net basic 2500" set; maybe add some that are missing.
There is no way any 1000 word vocab can handle even the most
basic use by beginners. (But 6000 is too many to learn.)
Esperanto started with around 750 but quickly grew.
The "bad" words can be ignored, and eventually forgotten.

From: Paul O Bartlett <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>

On Thu, 27 Jul 2000, Robin Gaskell wrote (excerpt):

>    . ~gene~ does seem the natural VERB to the Passive Voice, even
>    . ~though to say ~gene ge-X~ [=to be Xed] does seem like a
>    . ~mouthful,

    Strange.  Many languages with a somewhat isolating morphology form
passive constructions with some form of a verb like 'to be'.  What is
wrong with 'es ge-X'?  It seems to me that the 'gene ge-X' is modelled
on British dialects of English.  My experience has been that Americans
and users of British dialect use "get" rather differently, so that the
'gene ge-X' construction is merely an importation from British English.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Paul O Bartlett <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>
Date: Fri, 28 Jul 2000 21:16:47 -0400 (EDT)

On Fri, 28 Jul 2000, Robin Gaskell wrote:

> At 20:01 23/07/2000 -0500, Patrick Dunn wrote:
> > [...]

> *   This is how I understand it, too.  Forming a participle, by using a
> simple pre-fix, is a good pragmatic solution.

    Auxiliary language design involves a lot of trade-offs.  Slavish
adherence to some single principle at all costs, come what may, may
result in an inferior product.  For example, one Esperantist criticism
of Ido's plural construction in '-i' is that it creates an inflection
instead of an agglutination, and some Esperantists insist that only
agglutination should be allowed, come hell or high water.  I am not
attempting to mediate that dispute, but only to point out that some
designers have been willing to bend some one principle on other
grounds.  To say that Glosa must *ABSOLUTELY* eschew anything which
even seems to smack of a dreaded inflection seems to me to be merely
foolish.  Something may be beautiful as a paper theory, but in the real
world something else may actually be more practical.

On Fri, 28 Jul 2000, Charles wrote (excerpt):

> But the final vowels hardly ever matter, and so don't really
> need to be spelled "right".

    Although there are a small handful of word pairs differing only by
the terminal vowel.

>                             They'll all sound like schwa anyway.

    Possibly only from the mouths of us native English speakers, who
tend to blur and reduce our vowels.  Other users from different native
tongues may be much more precise in their vowel articulations.  I
suggest that native-speech sloppiness should not be an excuse for poor
articulation of an auxiliary language.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au

From: "Jay Bowks" <jjbowks{at}adam{dot}cheshire{dot}net>
Date: Fri, 28 Jul 2000 21:28:22 -0400

>From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
> But the final vowels hardly ever matter, and so don't really
> need to be spelled "right". They'll all sound like schwa anyway.
I disagree! Final vowels are very important. They help us to
understand words clearly. Just because English and French do
something of the sort does not at all mean that it is desirable.
Actually the lack of clear final vowel confuses the sound. To
a native speaker of Spanish, Portuguese, Italian etc. the lack
of distintc vowel sounds in English means that everything sounds
like it's mumbled. Vowels help to make communication easier.
Since,
Jay B.

The Richmond standard is ~gene X~ [to be X-ed].

~Mi pa gene nati, meno tet~  [I was born in April]

Literally, "I did get birth, month four."

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick_hempshall{at}hotmail{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 29 Jul 2000 08:12:22 +0100

>         She is the running woman.
1>   Fe eqa u du-kurso femina.  -  She is the running woman.
2>   Fe eqa u femina; qi kurso.  -  She is the woman who runs.
3>   Fe eqa u femina; qi du kurso.  -  She is the woman who is running.
4>   Fe eqa u kurso femina.*      [unsatisfactory]

Interesting, that english version of number 2 suggests to me that
she is in the habit of running, which glosa would indicate explicitly
by ~more~. Also ~eqa~ seems to me to be to "formal", why not
use the more relaxed ~es~. I don't see any problem with the
construction in number 4.

>
>          He, running, enters the street.
>   An, kurso, ki-in u strada.  -  He, running, enters the street.
>   An, du-kurso, ki-in u strada.  -  He, running enters the street.
>   Du-kurso, an ki-in u strada.  -  Running, he enters the street.
>   Kurso, an ki-in u strada.*    [Seems incomplete]
>   In u strada, an veni, kurso.  -  Into the street, he comes running.
>   In u strada, an veni, du-kurso.  - Into the street he comes running.

Seems a somewhat artifical thing to say. Why not just say
~ An dromo ad-in strata.~

For me, the various Glosa 1000 lists are the heart of Glosa, and should not
be
radically altered. Only where mistakes or contradictions are found, should
changes
be made. Leave the rest to evolution by actual usage.

I don't feel the same about Glosa 6000, which I think is just a registration
of the Greek
and Latin elements in terms already in use by science. This makes them
legitimately part
of Mega Glosa, like any other "international" word (such as those in the
Interlingua
IED), if presented in Glosa form. Such words are available, if the speaker
or writer
feels the need to use them.

I don't think we are free to trawl the Greek and Latin dictionaries for
words, but
should only use the elements of words that are in "international" use. Of
course, it
is true that people don't know what these elements mean. They are just
familiar
patterns of sounds. Learning Glosa gives them meaning. This explains the
sci-tech
words that we have up to now taken as wholes, and so makes Glosa memorable.


>     Before there are radical changes, however, Wendy Ashby must be

Mi suggesti qi Glosa habe u seqe forma:-
U HOLO VERBA GRUPO:- Andro dona avi a gina
U HOLO VERBA GRUPO (H):- Un alti andro pa gentili dona u pusi avi a kali gina
Il es 4 mero in u H. Andro/un alti andro + dona/pa gentili dona + avi/u pusi
avi + a gina/a kali gina
Plu mero es U KLAVI VERBA GRUPO (K) (dona/pa gentili dona), U STI NIMA VERBA
GRUPO (S) (andro/un alti andro), PLU PO KLAVI VERBA GRUPO 1 e 2 (P1 ) (avi/u
pusi avi) e (P2) (a gina/a kali gina)
U norma verba seri in glosa es S K alo S K P1 alo S K P1 P2
U komanda nece omite S.
Na ski qi andro pa sti u akti, u avi habe u akti pa akti ad id, u akti pa es
u dona e u akti pa fini a u gina.
Pro idio efekti, u seqe pote ge-muta.
Avi ge-dona a gina per andro.
Pluso, hetera seqe possi :- "A gina, avi per andro pa dona" sed rare , posi
S es u nima grupo (NG):- id habe u "u"verba (UV) "u", u nima verba (NV)
"andro" e posi plu auxi verba (AV) "alti". U seqe sio UV + AV + NV "u kali
gina".
K es homo S e habe PF (pa/fu) + AV (gentili) + KV (dona)
UV e PF fu face ma facili u ski de signifikati de H.
P1 alo P2 pote es NVG, AVG alo DVG (de +NVG) "a plu gina" "a u gina" "a gina"
*
*


I suggest that to make Glosa clearer and easier to understand, that
A standard Glosa sentence could be looked upon as having 4 phrases:- verb
phrase, and to start with in some sentences, 2 preposition phrases and a noun
phrase, for instance, VP=pa dona, NP=u avi, PP1=a gina and PP2= per andro.
The order would not matter as the subject would be PP2, the object NP, the
indirect object PP1 and VP could be at the end. In fact any order would work.
If we all agreed to standardize we could trim the "per" off and have
subjectNP   VP  object  NP and then PP1. I would personally prefer the
symmetry of NP VP NP to either VP NP NP or NP NP VP and I think that
separating the 2 NPs would make the sentence more easily understood. Adding
PP1 at the end would not disturb the symmetry very much. To give variety or
emphasis the order could be varied to NP(old object)u avi VP+"ge"(to signal
the reversal of the positions of object and subject). We now have
possibilities to complete the sentence:- full stop or PP1 or PP2 or both PP1
and PP2 in either order (the English passive can be very useful). The "ge" is
derived from the word "gene" and, as far as I can see could be said to have
little to do with the "ge-" of German.
In Glosa, a phrase seems to have 3 parts, an introductory small word (IW) at
the beginning, the head word (HW) at the end and various bits of adjectival
or adverbial gubbins in the middle(MG).
U + alti + andro. HW is usually followed by IW of the next phrase. Where this
is so, it is easy to distinguish the phrases and IW identifies the phrase's
function. NP, PP,VP etc. The IP of NP could be "u", "plu", "multi" or a
numeral, of VP, "pa", "fu", "du" etc. I personally would like an IP for the
present tense i.e. "pres" or "pi" for present indicative. The IP of a PP
would be a preposition and, as this will be followed by a NP, the word "u"
could be amalgamated into the prep and be omitted .
Adjectival gubbins would need to be in the apropriate NP or it would be
difficult which NP was being gubbinized . On the other hand, as there is only
1 VP an adverbial gubbins could be more or less anywhere and being placed
away from the VP would shorten and simplify the actual sentence.

The order NP  VP  NP PP seems to be the norm as Glosa was initially developed
in England but other orders could be acceptable if it were clear what was to
follow.
(u orto glosa-V--)
u alti andro pa gentili dona plu pusi avi a kali gina.
(glosa neo u---V)
avi pusi plu gina kali a andro alti u dona gentili.        Present tense
implied
(glosa u neo V----)
dona gentili pri gina a kali andro per alti avi plu pusi    pri=present tense
(u orta glosa)
u alto andro du gentila dona plu pusi avi a kala gina. I like this one -
rhyming phrasesâ¦â¦.
syd

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge- (Writing)
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sat, 29 Jul 2000 10:14:44 -0700


> I suggest that to make Glosa clearer and easier to understand, that
> A standard Glosa sentence could be looked upon as having 4 phrases:- verb
> phrase, and to start with in some sentences, 2 preposition phrases and a noun
> phrase, for instance, VP=pa dona, NP=u avi, PP1=a gina and PP2= per andro.

Here is a better quasi-scientific notation which
captures the essential syntax you are trying to
describe informally, now using a rough BN form:

sentence:    (prepphrases) subject predicate
subject:     nounphrase
prepphrases: preposition nounphrase
nounphrase:  (determiner) (adjectives) noun (prepphrases)
predicate:   verbphrase (nounphrase) (prepphrases)
verbphrase:  (auxiliaries) verb

auxiliaries:  pa, nu, du, es, etc.
determiner:   u, plu, u-la, etc.
prepositions: de, ad, peri, etc.

> The order would not matter as the subject would be PP2, the object NP, the
> indirect object PP1 and VP could be at the end. In fact any order would work.

This is not true; Glosa is very dependent on phrase order
within clauses and words within phrases. I would prefer
to force the object to always immediately follow the verb,
and the subject to always immediately precede, though
it may have its own "heavy modifiers" intervening.
In Glosa as in English, light modifiers (the articles,
quantifiers, adjectives) go to the left of the noun,
and heavies go to the right.

Prepositional phrases (and subclauses, treated the same)
must always modify their immediate predecessor. Otherwise
you get "attachment ambiguities" as in English which are
unacceptable in a well-defined syntax. A sentence-initial
prepphrase is "adverbial", modifying the whole sentence,
and a verb-following prepphrase is an "oblique object",
i.e. not a direct object but closely related to the verb.
Lone adverbs can also act as prepositional phrases.
The above scheme could be modified to show that:

sentence: (sentencemodifier) subject predicate
sentencemodifier: prepphrases
sentencemodifier: adverb
predicate: verbphrase (object) (obliques)
object: nounphrase (prepphrases)
obliques: prepphrases

This sort of thing is excellently described in Rick
Morneau's essay on syntax: http://srv.net/~ram/syntax.html

A subject is not always the doer of the verb's action.
It is always the highlighted "star" of the sentence.
Passive voice was invented to enable swapping like this:

"dog bit man"       ... talking mainly about the dog, and subject=doer
"man is bit by dog" ... the man is more important, but object=doer
"man is bitten"     ... there is no doer (agent)
"dog bit            ... there is no doee (patient)

In English it can get confusing and amusing:
"the dog bit the man in the closet/butt."

Other languages would use other patterns, but Glosa is
really a simplified and hopefuly regularized English
syntax with relexed Greco-Latin vocabulary. There can be
but little latitude in the word order without catastrophe.
Ido and Occidental use part-of-speech marking to safely
allow such variation, but that is not the Glosa way.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] About Glosa and Russian
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sat, 29 Jul 2000 13:33:35 -0700

Thinking about core vocabulary some more ... I found this
by searching the Linguist List for "topic dictionary":

http://listserv.linguistlist.org/cgi-bin/wa?A2=ind9512D&L=linguist&P=R4044

Roget's Thesaurus and many other resources were discussed.
Roget's was used by Ron Clark to devise the Glosa vocab,
but the details or methodology are unknown to me.
One of the other items was Steven Glazier's "word menu",
which I bought and find very useful.

I would like to have Glosa vocab categorized in a similar
manner. Any systematic method is good, among other reasons
because it can expose holes or redundancies in the vocabulary.
In an online database (I mean just a simple multicolumn list)
multiple categorizations can be accomodated simultaneously.
For instance, a horse might be "animal; vehicle; food" ...

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] a more flexible phrase order
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sun, 30 Jul 2000 15:13:52 -0700


The recent suggestion to allow noun phrases to float more
freely than permitted by Glosa's strict SVO order, is much too
tempting for this recovering conlanger to resist any longer.
Although it would stretch or break the creoleness of Glosa,
it might happen, if Glosa were to evolve/drift/degenerate
into a natlang in some alternate universe.

First the preposition "per" (or some other, maybe "ex")
would become obligatory in Glosa-2 for marking agent/doer,
as is already allowed marginally in the Glosa of now:

G1:   kani pa morda u andro         ... the usual "active voice"
G1:   andro es ge-morda per kani    ... the less common "passive"

G2e:  per kani pa morda u andro     ... "ergative" becomes mandatory
G2e:  andro pa morda per kani       ... new improved passive, no "es ge-"
G2e:  per kani u andro pa morda     ... SOV etc orders now possible

Meanwhile, a different ("nominative") dialect of Neo-Glosa
marks the object/patient with "em", instead of the agent:

G2n:  pa morda u kani em andro      ... VSO version

Being human, they make war for this or something equally important.
A subtle mixing, as in England the Danes mixed with Anglo-Saxons,
now results. Order is free at last and there is peace for a time:

G3:   in horti per kani em andro pa morda
G3:   pa morda em andro per kani in horti

This demonstrates how the price of free constituent order
is a greatly increased difficulty in parsing (mentally)
even the simplest sentences, exactly as in Esperanto-1.
Cute, though.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] dictionary, was Re: fingertalk (for deaf)
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sun, 30 Jul 2000 22:49:51 -0700


> *   There is a sadness about this, for me.  I still use the Glosa 6000 book
> as my ready reference.  If the consensus is for major revisions of the
> dictionary,

No need for major changes, and no need to revise the G-6000.
Only a change in "policy": a word still ought to be spelled
as defined in official references, *but* the final vowels are
merely cosmetic, so variant spellings of finals are "acceptable"
and will not be used to differentiate any new word pairs.
Never tell learners they're "wrong" because of a silly final;
especially since the authors weren't consistent themselves!

The new Net-2500 should carefully select from among the "best" words,
avoiding pairs e.g. bali/bale. Any confusion would dwindle with time;
my guess is that 99% of Glosa users will never even see the G-6000.
So-called "backward compatibility" is a good thing sometimes ...
as long as it doesn't stop progress.

> *   So, I would suggest that a revision, a possible Glosa Ver_3, might come
> out first as the 2500-word Learner's Dictionary.  It might have to be
> prined in Australia, as I know that Wendy has no spare money for the
> production of a new dictionary;

Specialty printing using personal computers is now a lot easier
and higher-quality than it was; I think a good Net-2500 list
can attract a huge new audience, and only a small percentage
of those need to order the nicer (new and old) printed materials
to make it viable.

Mi suggesti qi Glosa habe u seqe forma:-
U HOLO VERBA GRUPO:- Andro dona avi a gina
U HOLO VERBA GRUPO (H):- Un alti andro pa gentili dona u pusi avi a kali gina
Il es 4 mero in u H. Andro/un alti andro + dona/pa gentili dona + avi/u pusi
avi + a gina/a kali gina
Plu mero es U KLAVI VERBA GRUPO (K) (dona/pa gentili dona), U STI NIMA VERBA
GRUPO (S) (andro/un alti andro), PLU PO KLAVI VERBA GRUPO 1 e 2 (P1 ) (avi/u
pusi avi) e (P2) (a gina/a kali gina)
U norma verba seri in glosa es S K alo S K P1 alo S K P1 P2
U komanda nece omite S.
Na ski qi andro pa sti u akti, u avi habe u akti pa akti ad id, u akti pa es
u dona e u akti pa fini a u gina.
Pro idio efekti, u seqe pote ge-muta.
Avi ge-dona a gina per andro.
Pluso, hetera seqe possi :- "A gina, avi per andro pa dona" sed rare , posi
S es u nima grupo (NG):- id habe u "u"verba (UV) "u", u nima verba (NV)
"andro" e posi plu auxi verba (AV) "alti". U seqe sio UV + AV + NV "u kali
gina".
K es homo S e habe PF (pa/fu) + AV (gentili) + KV (dona)
UV e PF fu face ma facili u ski de signifikati de H.
P1 alo P2 pote es NVG, AVG alo DVG (de +NVG) "a plu gina" "a u gina" "a gina"

I suggest that to make Glosa clearer and easier to understand, that
A standard Glosa sentence could be looked upon as having 4 phrases:- verb
phrase, and to start with in some sentences, 2 preposition phrases and a noun
phrase, for instance, VP=pa dona, NP=u avi, PP1=a gina and PP2= per andro.
The order would not matter as the subject would be PP2, the object NP, the
indirect object PP1 and VP could be at the end. In fact any order would work.
If we all agreed to standardize we could trim the "per" off and have
subjectNP   VP  object  NP and then PP1. I would personally prefer the
symmetry of NP VP NP to either VP NP NP or NP NP VP and I think that
separating the 2 NPs would make the sentence more easily understood. Adding
PP1 at the end would not disturb the symmetry very much. To give variety or
emphasis the order could be varied to NP(old object)u avi VP+"ge"(to signal
the reversal of the positions of object and subject). We now have
possibilities to complete the sentence:- full stop or PP1 or PP2 or both PP1
and PP2 in either order (the English passive can be very useful). The "ge" is
derived from the word "gene" and, as far as I can see could be said to have
little to do with the "ge-" of German.
In Glosa, a phrase seems to have 3 parts, an introductory small word (IW) at
the beginning, the head word (HW) at the end and various bits of adjectival
or adverbial gubbins in the middle(MG).
U + alti + andro. HW is usually followed by IW of the next phrase. Where this
is so, it is easy to distinguish the phrases and IW identifies the phrase's
function. NP, PP,VP etc. The IP of NP could be "u", "plu", "multi" or a
numeral, of VP, "pa", "fu", "du" etc. I personally would like an IP for the
present tense i.e. "pres" or "pi" for present indicative. The IP of a PP
would be a preposition and, as this will be followed by a NP, the word "u"
could be amalgamated into the prep and be omitted .
Adjectival gubbins would need to be in the apropriate NP or it would be
difficult which NP was being gubbinized . On the other hand, as there is only
1 VP an adverbial gubbins could be more or less anywhere and being placed
away from the VP would shorten and simplify the actual sentence.

The order NP  VP  NP PP seems to be the norm as Glosa was initially developed
in England but other orders could be acceptable if it were clear what was to
follow.
(u orto glosa-V--)
u alti andro pa gentili dona plu pusi avi a kali gina.
(glosa neo u---V)
avi pusi plu gina kali a andro alti u dona gentili.        Present tense
implied
(glosa u neo V----)
dona gentili pri gina a kali andro per alti avi plu pusi    pri=present tense
(u orta glosa)
u alto andro du gentila dona plu pusi avi a kala gina. I like this one -
rhyming phrasesâ¦â¦.
syd
there shd hv been pictures but they'v disappeared!

At 13:05 26/07/2000 +0930, Michael wrote:
>Thinking of   .....   redundancy   .....  Helper wheels.
>
>They may be a help because  ...  the market of
>an IAL is always beginners, rather than native speakers.
>
>They may be a hindrance because   .....  Redundancy means that it doesn't
>matter if you get a few things wrong.  Still, if there is more stuff you
>feel you "have" to get right, that is a problem.
>
*   So, if a language has a minor degree of redundancy, this could be a
good thing for learners.  I agree.  Glosa was designed to have virtually no
redundancy, but a little snuck in.  This made it more 'human' and less rigid.

>The Glosa tense marking seems to have an advantage over inflexions
>which have no redundancy.  It's not as easy to be wrong with "pa",
>you put it there or you don't.  It's easier to be wrong if the
>present ending is "-as" and the past ending is "-is", a single
>vowel change, easy to overlook.
>
*   As a one-time teacher, I can confirm this difference: tell the kids
that the words differ simply by an "X" and a "Y" then you are asking for
trouble; you are triggering them for error.  Glosa's no inflection policy
does make the modifying particles much easier to recognise and remember.
    What's more, PA, DU, FU and NU all feature a distinctively different
consonant.  I have always thought of it as a ''Meccano'' language, and have
found it as easy to construct, because the bits look and sound so different.

>In practice, is this a real advantage, or a real problem?  I guess
>it doesn't make much difference.
>
*   Wrong guess!
    When the neophyte speaks an analytical languasge, he can always pause
.. to gather his thoughts .. between words; but, when using the
agglutinative, synthetic-type language, he feels like an idiot pausing
once, or twice, during the delivery of one, highly inflected word!

>If inflexions were banished from Engish we might have something
>like:  The ing move finger do write and after did write do move
>on.  In some languages the adjective may optionally precede or
>follow the noun.   
*   Now we're getting silly.
    English will never be reformed, or have usages banished.
    Using English 'inflections' as "words" is senseless, because they
developed into their present form over a long period, and do not make sense 
in this "analytic" form.  What's more the syntax analysis used in
Functional Grammar makes more sense than your attempt, here.

>        The tense marker, adverb or whatver it is could
>do the same, so we could have "moveing" as well as "ing move".
>
*   My researches come to the conclusion that although there seems an
endless variation in the way we can use English, this is not so, and each
of the possible alternatives has its own feel for 'the proper syntax.'
    The better, published writers all tend to use the same set of syntax
rules.
    Glosa has a reasonably well defined set of syntax rules, too; however,
it has been designed to offer less variation in the turn of phrase.

>To put it another way:  The anta move finger as write and after
>is write as move on.  If there is a present marker as well as a
>past marker there is no advantage over inflexion.  It is necessary
>to say:  The ing move finger write and after did write move on.
>
*   As "He runs," can be little else than a present statement, ~An kurso,~
is also a clear-cut present tense statement.  It is just the bare
Infinitive form.
    As, again, in English, we can stress the actual moment of the present,
so too, in Glosa, we can extend, or modify, the meaning of the present:-
 EG   He is running now.   -->  An du nu kurso.
                            continues now to run
No sweat. No inflection. Simple pre-particles lead us into the VERB. 
    This idea, of having the substantive word at the end of the phrase, is
called Head Final structure; and, in Glosa, it applies to both Noun Phrases
and Verb Phrases.  With a reasonably well-defined heirarchy of function
words leading up to the final substantive word, this gives Glosa a clearly
distinctive, and readily recognisable, phrasal structure.   
    For the reason of demarking phrase and clause boundaries, punctuation,
in Glosa, has grammatical signiificance.  And I hasten to add, that in
non-seen, spoken utterance, the very same boundaries are denoted by vocal
inflection. 
    I send this posting in an attempt to say that the transcription of
idiomatic English does NOT make Glosa.

    On turning the coin over, we do find that good Glosa does, for the most
part, transcribe into grammatically correct English; and, without pushing
the comparison too far, well-written, literal English usually does 
transcribe into good Glosa. 

    Over recent years my English writing style has been improving.
Although this could be a simple case of ^try, try again,^ I tend to relate
the recent thinking I apply to the turning of my English-language phrases, 
to the mental effort that I have been putting into improving my Glosa.
    So, Glosa could be good for you.

Saluta,
  Robin
    P.S.  The idea of Head Final structure is spelt out with Glosa examples
in the attachd Seminar #1.   R.

_A first Seminar on Glosa____________________________R. Gaskell Sept,1995_
               (With apologies, in English)
WORDS
        Words in Glosa represent concepts, and are really `concept/words.'
They represent ideas, but, depending on their position in the sentence,
and their relationships to other words, they may represent that idea in its
solid form (noun), active form (verb), or as a modifier of another idea
(adjective, adverb).

        The words do not change for reasons of grammar (system for getting
understanding from words): this means that words can be used, within
reason, as any part of speech, but have no letters added to indicate this
useage (no Part-of-Speech markers); also, the words which act as verbs,
`verbs', have no endings added to them (no inflections) to give added
understanding of how, or when, the action is taken.

        The order of the words (syntax) gives us the meaning in Glosa; the
language can be described as having `syntax-based grammar.'

PHRASES
        The phrase (small group of words) is the basic unit of sentence
construction in Glosa.  A small group of words might include a thing and
some description of it (noun phrase), or the group might include an action
and some modifications to this action (verb phrase).  A small group of
words at the start of a Glosa sentence might qualify the action of the
sentence (adverbial phrase).

        Within each phrase, each word is modified by the one before it, and
there is a gradual increase in importance of the words, with the main
concept/word (`noun' or `verb') as the last word of the phrase.
                e.g.  the three fast, loud red cars                  NP
                      plu tri celero fo-sono rubi vagona

                e.g.  were quickly end excitedly talking loudly      VP
                      pa du celero e excita fo-sono dice

                e.g.  while running     (relates to  --->  verb)     AdvP
                      tem kursi

        In speech, phrases are said as one group, often with a raising then
lowering of intonation (pitch), from start to finish; in written form, if
it seems necessary for the understanding of meaning, the start and finish
of phrases, is indicated using punctuation (commas).

CLAUSES
        A clause is a larger group of words containing two (NP + VP) or
more phrases, and such a simple two or three phrase clause can make up the
whole sentence (major clause).  However, a clause - which always includes
at least one verb phrase - might add meaning to the action of the
sentence (Adverbial Clause), might give more description to one of the
things in a sentence (Adjectival Clause), or it could be a group of words,
including a `verb,' used in the place that you would normally find a noun
phrase (Noun Clause).

      e.g.  The boy arrived.              U ju-an pa ariva.            MC

      e.g.  The girl went home.           U ju-fe pa ki a fe domi.     MC

      e.g.  The man will eat the meal.    Un andra fu vora u vora.     MC

      e.g.  She spoke so loudly that she was heard.
            Fe pa ta sono dice ke fe pa gene ge-audi.                AdvC
                          ~~~~ <-- ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

      e.g.  The girl went home after she had eaten a good meal.
            U ju-fe pa ki a fe domi, po fe pra vora u boni vora.     AdvC
                       ~~   <---     ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
      e.g.  The boy, who was fat and badly dressed, arrived.
            U ju-an; qi pa es paki e mali ge-vesti, pa ariva.        AdjC
              ~~~~~ <-- ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
      e.g.  I like to get away from the house.
                  (getting)
            Mi amo gene ab u domi.                                     NC
                   ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
      e.g.  The farmer hoped that it would rain.
            Un agri-pe pa spera: ke id sio pluvi.                      NC
                       ~~~(V)~~  ~~~~(O)~~~~~~~~

SEQUENCE IN A CLAUSE
        The normal sequence in Glosa is the same as in English:-
 Main Clause =   noun phrase    -    verb phrase     -    noun phrase
                  (SUBJECT)            (VERB)              (OBJECT)
            Doer of the action    Action being done    Thing acted upon
           e.g.     He                 posts               a letter.
                    An                 posta               u grama.

        There are three main variations to this: clauses with no object
(intransitive verb); clauses where, for reasons of style, the speaker,
or writer, wishes to emphasise the object by placing it first (Passive
Voice); and Noun Clauses which have no subject (infinitive verb).
           e.g.    She (NP)    sang. (VP)                 Fe pa kanta.
                 (SUBJECT)    (VERB)

           e.g.   The letter       was posted        by the man.
                   U grama      pa gene ge-posta     ex un andra.
                               (did get got-post)
                   (OBJECT)         (VERB)                (SUBJECT)

           e.g.                  - running (=to run)     on wet grass
                                    kursi              epi ge-hidra gra
                                    (VERB)                 (OBJECT)

        Because its grammar depends on the word order, Glosa works best if
we keep to the normal (S-V-O) structure in clauses.  In poetry and fiction,
however, the stylistic variations are necessary.

FUNCTION WORDS
        These are the small words that are not concept/words, but which
join these words together (conjunctions), lead us into a phrase or clause
(prepositions), create negatives, introduce questions, or modify the timing
of an action when placed before a `verb' (tense particles).  There are also
the small words placed before `nouns' to tell us that they are things, and
how many, or how much, of them there are (determinants).
        Joining words:  e, sed, pluso, alo, ni

        Prepositions: tem, a, ex, de, anti, seqe, po, pre, vice, krom ....

        Negatives: ne, nuli, no-, ni ... ni, nuli-

        Questions: Qe, qestio, qod, qo-

        Tense particles: pa, fu, du, nu, pra, sio, ge-, gene

        Before `nouns': u, plu, plura, uno, vario, poli, oligo, u mero de
         (determinant)

SENTENCES
        Putting all the above together, in Glosa, as in English, sentences
can be made up of various combinations of phrases and clauses - as long as
the Subject-Verb-Object rule is observed.  In Glosa, also, there is the
added requirement that words in phrases follow the rule of increasing
importance, with the main concept/word last.

        If the sentence is made up of phrases only, there is a single
`verb', and the sentence is described as a Major Clause.  If there are two
or more `verbs', then the `verb' describing the main action forms the Main
Clause, and the other clauses (NC, AdvC, AdjC) are called Subordinate
Clauses.  The whole sentence can, however, still be called the Major
Clause.
        The secret of good sentence formation is in using well-formed
phrases to make up the sentences and their constituent clauses.

PRECEDENCE IN PHRASES
RULE: In a phrase, a word is modified by the word in front of it (its
        precedent.)
RULE: In a normal sentence (Major Clause), there is a tendency for the
        first noun phrase (SUBJECT) to modify the verb phrase (VERB), and
        then, for the verb phrase to modify the second noun phrase (OBJECT).
RULE: A modifying clause (Adjectival or Adverbial) follows directly after
        the concept/word that it modifies; this structure is indicated
        by the placing of a semi-colon (;) or possibly a colon (:) before
        the new clause.

 In a noun phrase, the sequence is:-

           determinant  -  number  -  quantity  -  modifier(s)  -  noun

        e.g. plu tri no-ge-numera, fo sono, no-puri ju-an
             the three uncounted, shouting, dirty boys

           Note.  The concept/word used as a noun is the only element of
              a noun phrase that is essential.

 In a verb phrase, the sequence is:-
                                                    |   verb
  negative - tense - modifier(s) - auxiliary verb - |      OR
 particle                                           | verboid + amplifier

                     e.g. ne pa hedo, no-soni tenta | kursi
                                                    | ki ana

                       did not happily, quietly try | to run
                                                    | to go up

           Note, again.  The concept/word used as a verb is the only
               element of a verb phrase that is essential.

 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Mon, 31 Jul 2000 18:29:00 +1000

At 12:02 26/07/2000 +0930, Michael Talbot-Wilson wrote:
>
>> I like the idea of a pure-ideal pidgin/creole as IAL.
>
>Using separate words in place of the number inflexion of the noun
>or the tense inflexions of the verb seems perfectly obvious and
>easy.  But I'm not certain that there is any advantage in it.  
>
*   It can depend on cultural and psychological factors, and may not have
anything to do with logic.
    However, there are patterns in common in English and the other more
successful national languages: we are still struggling with the idea of a
"Universal Grammar."

>That Glosa claims it but doesn't fully do it is a bit strange.  
>And after Peano, Interlingua grew grammar, also a bit strange.
>
*   Glosa is 99% uninflected: well within the realms of scientific
significance.
    The Intelingua people back-slid.

>And regular inflexions are also perfectly obvious and easy.  Isn't
>this fact implied by the other one?  Is there any essential
>difference?
*   Though I have not understood you perfectly, I would say that both
inflected and non-inflected languages incorporate the same functions, but,
physically, achieve them differently.
    And although I have not studied it, I do understand that Signed
Language is also "inflected", and this is developed, naturally, by young
people using the standard Signs.  So, a tendency towards inflection 'could'
be a part of the universal language story.
    In terms of the tense inflections of VERBS, Glosa achieves this quite
well with particles.  Some other aspects are achieved with separate words,
such as the conditionality of ~sio~.
      
>    Isn't is purely a matter of orthography?  If it is
>agreed that the "ge-" prefix is an inflexion (is it?), is anything
>achieved by making "ge" an autonomous word, and pretending we have
>dispensed with inflexions?
>
*   As I explained in an earlier post, the Passiive Voice arrived late in
Glosa's development, and the authors decided the neatest way of adding it
was by invoking a participle.  Thus GENE -> GE- was born.
    And, yes, purists label this as an "inflection."  Work it out for
yourself: if the "-en" of "woken" and the "-ed" of "chased" in ..
"I was woken and chased by a wild bull," are ''inflections'' then so is the
~ge-~ of ~ge-leve~ in ~An gene ge-leve per u fo soni explode.~
    So, why, you might ask, is it hyphenated?  Because, in Glosa,
pronunciation is with the penultimate vowel (or the final vowel, if there
is only one), thus ~geleve~ and ~ge-leve~ sound different; and, for
practical purposes, might be quite different words!

>Entire avoidance of morphological change in derivation is more
>problematic for me.  Do you not lose clarity or precision at this
>point, unless supported by numerous traditional phrases?
>
*   This is a true problem: in some cases we have such derivations built
in, as in ~kapitala~ [capital] and ~kapitalismo~ [capitalism] plus
~sociali~ and ~socialismo~; but if you were to derive a new word, I would
ask you to hyphenate. 
 EG   sustine   -   sustain
      sustin-abili   -   sustainable
      sustin-abili-ra   -   sustainability
  <Yes, I did look it up in the Latin dictionary.  It was not as
fashionable a decade ago, when the Glosa 6000 was put together, as it is 
now.  It IS our responsibility to keep the Glosa lexicon current.>

   Linguistically the hyphens are necessary both as a guide to
pronunciation and derivation; and they seem part of the language.  Were I
to write ~sustinabilira~ the word would look undecipherable, and the stress
of pronunciation would be on the penultimate "I" which is NOT stressed in
the pronunciation of the correctly derived, compound word.

>If I say "Quick!", you need context to begin to know whether I
>mean "Come quick!" or "Wasn't that quick!".  If I say "Quickly!",
>perhaps it is just idiom that excludes "Didn't he come quickly!"  
>Or maybe it is an obscure rule of phraseology.  Do we need a
>catalog of such rules, to mirror them in the pure ideal
>pidgin/creole?
>
*   If we need rules they are the simple pragmatic rules of combining
pronunciation with the excision of unwanted terminal vowels, combined with
hyphenation.  However, Glosa will be used in context, as is English, so the
sorts of problems, above, will be substantially the same for Glosa as they
are for English.

>Maybe a change of word form in derivation is just helper wheels.  
>But when you can't ride, aren't helper wheels nice!"
>
*   As I see it the hyphens are the ^trainer wheels.^
    However, there are two basic forms of Glosa, the highly elided, and the
completely unelided: beginners would learn the language better, by, at the
start, throwing everything in,  EG
   An age vagona.   ...  He drove.    He drives.    He drives the car.
   [he drive (the car)]

   Un andra pa age u vagona.   ...   The man drove the car.
  [the man did drive the car]  

Saluta, 
  Robin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Michael Talbot-Wilson <mtw{at}birdseye{dot}view{dot}net{dot}au>
Date: Mon, 31 Jul 2000 23:05:22 +0930 (CST)

Robin Gaskell wrote:

>     While Ron Clark had a preference for the Greek roots, I try to remain

That's probably not fair (or too fair) to Ron Clark.  Wasn't the
inclusion of Greek roots a key part of the vision of Bodmer/
Hogben?

One of their arguments, I think, was that it would assist the
inclusion of scientific words in the future language.

Some other IALs have chosen roots from the languages most largely
spoken in Europe this (20th) century.  What is most common among
them often turns out to have come from Latin (because of the
combined weight of English and the romance languages), so they get
about the same outcome as the Interlingua policy of taking direct
from Latin.

But also, since science coinings are generally international, and
often Greek-based, that approach of taking from modern languages
seems to achieve the objective that was hoped to come from
directly Greek-based roots.

> neutral in this; however, I do definitely enjoy the availability, in the
> broader application of the language, of a prime word from one Classical
> source, and a synonym from the other.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] dictionary, was Re: fingertalk (for deaf)*
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>

Marcel's sample of English words beginning in RO:

roam, roar, roast, rob, robe, robot, robin,
rock, rocket, rod, rogue, roll, romance,
roof, room, root, rope, rose, rot,
rough, round, rouse, route, routine,
rove, row boat, row, royal

Kilgariff's words in RO:

road n
roar v
rob v, robbery n
rock n
rock v
rocket n
rod n
role n
roll n
roll v
romance n, romantic a
roof n
room n
root n, root v
rope n
rose n
rotation n
rotten a
rough a, roughly adv
round a, round adv, round n, round prep, round v
route n
routine n
row n
row v
royal a, royalty n

So by my rough count, the M-list includes 19 of 25 K-items.
This approximates a good practical word list for English-Glosa.

We could be sure that if the K-list were fully equivalenced,
there would be *no* *holes* in the Glosa vocabulary, because
the BNC is the largest corpus of English ever assembled,
and the K-list covers over 90% of word usage by frequency.
Yet, this requires much less than 6000 Glosa roots.
No other auxlang/IAL has accomplished this sort of
systematic validation of its vocabulary.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] a more flexible phrase order
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Mon, 31 Jul 2000 10:22:27 -0700

Sydpidd{at}aol{dot}com wrote:

> Incidentally is there a way of removing
> grotty emails from the list?

Why worry?

> I'm still sruggling to get a web page done - In
> AOL is the remote filename the name of a file on

"source/local" = your own personal computer
"remote" = AOL web server

I already commented about word order, basically
you have to be very careful and can't change
very much without breaking it, except that you can
often move prepositional phrases around a bit,
but even there you can get ambiguity as to what
the preposition attaches to:

If "I shot the dog in the forest", what exactly
was in the forest? Several possibilities.
English is rife with attachment ambiguities.
But in Glosa I expect "in" to modify only "dog".

I do think a new direct object preposition "em" would
nicely accomplish much of what you suggest, though.
And indirect object should *never* lack a preposition.
Then you could freely move the "per/ad/em" phrases.
Then, the only safe place for adverbial phrases would
be the very front or immediately following the verb.
And it's already mostly like that, so why not?
